B005OK81HA|3.0|This new Scorpions release is a mix of 7 re recordings of songs from their classic 1980-1991 period and 6 cover songs.  While the Scorpions have more than earned the right to do whatever they want, I find this release to be ok but unessential. First the issue of Re Recording. As the re recordings are of classic songs with nothing really new revealed in them, I say what's the point of redoing a classic? Take any of the first 7 songs, why would we want to hear them redone?  The originals are all classics in their own right.  Secondly, the issue of the cover tunes.  While Children of the Revolution and All Day and All of the Night really do rock hard and sound great, I don't feel like any new ground is really broken.  The covers of Across the Universe or Tainted Love really didn't do it for me.  In summary, I am not sure what listener this is aimed at.  If you are fairly new to the Scorpions and want some or all of these classic hits, buy one of the greatest hits that are available by the band not this. At least you will have the originals and you probably won't care about hearing some cover tunes by the band.  If you are a fan of the \\"classic period\\" of the Scorpions (The Mercury Records Years), and want something by the band, Pick up their 2007 release Humanity Hour One and if you are unfamiliar with any of the studio albums prior to 1978's Tokyo Tapes definitely seek those out.  If you are a hardcore fan that picks up everything the band releases, then you probably own this already! I personally don't find anything new here that is essential.  This will be one that will probably go on the shelf and probably not come out often.

1594203334|5.0|I'm so happy to have read this book, even though there are plenty of things I enjoy about American parenting that I can't imagine giving up (breastfeeding without questioning my mental health, praise from my children's teachers.)  Druckerman is as thorough in her research about modern parenting in France as I can imagine anyone being.  She's the best person to have written the book -- it sounds like it took a lot of prodding for French parents to articulate what they are doing differently and only an outsider would have been able to see the differences for what they are.<br /><br />The reason I enjoyed this so much is because she is quick to admit where she simply could not do exactly as the French parents do.  If her kids never questioned her authority, never needed correction, never begged for snacks, I'd wonder whether this were all real.  But you'll hear about her transition from a certain kind of American parent to...less of a certain kind of American parent.  And I ended up liking her for it.<br /><br />Having said that, it's hard to relate to the Park Slope parents she writes about.  I don't see parenting to that extreme in my everyday life.  But there is enough truth there that reading another perspective on raising children feels healthy.  We can all debate whether this is \\"true\\" French parenting or, as I have, whether all American parents are as neurotic as those in cloistered areas in NY (they're not.)  Heck, we can even talk about her fitness as a writer (as another reviewer did) based upon an article she wrote for Marie Claire.<br /><br />But I think she did a fantastic job and I almost wish I could send her out to other countries to do the same sort of writing about parenting there, too.  Since that's not plausible,  I will just say I'd happily read more from her and I bet you will, too.

Her \\"sequel\\" to Pride and Prejudice, which begins some six years after the marriage of Elizabeth to Fitzwilliam Darcy echoes Austen's style so well that at times I had to remind myself whose work I was reading.<br /><br />Our story begins as Mrs. Darcy and her household are preparing for the annual ball in honour of Mr. Darcy's late mother, Lady Anne. Little do they know that Elizabeth's flighty sister Lydia (who, along with her husband Mr. Wickham, is persona non grata at Pemberley) is planning to crash the party.<br /><br />Lydia turns up at the Darcy's door in a speeding, barely controlled coach, screaming that her husband is dead. On their way to drop Lydia at Pemberley, Wickham and his friend Denny had a disagreement so intense that Denny had jumped out of the carriage and Wickham had gone after him. Hearing gunshots, Lydia had immediately assumed that Wickham had been killed and directed the driver to go immediately to Pemberley.<br /><br />I must confess to being somewhat disappointed that Lydia's husband was fine. It was Denny who was dead; being the only other person present, Wickham was charged with the crime. Such a turn of events caused all kinds of turmoil to this Janeite: is the admittedly immoral Wickham really capable of murder? 0061969656|3.0|I've been a big fan of Cornwell's other Saxon Tales books, so I looked forward to this newest entry. This book focuses on the period around the death of Alfred the Great in 899, and I suppose in many ways it was necessary to make its pacing quite a bit slower than the previous books. History is really to blame, because as the novel details the period immediately before and after Alfred's death was, surprisingly, relatively peaceful, which doesn't suit our erstwhile hero Uthred any more than it suited me as a reader. Cornwell does an admirable job of trying to get Uthred into trouble, but overall not a lot happens. The book is of course setting up the events to follow as Edward tries to unite all of England, and I suspect that the series will return to form soon enough. I'd recommend the novel to readers of the series, but it's definitely not one to give someone whom you hope to turn into a fan of Cornwell.

The novel moves on from there, told in a series of short flash-forwards to the present and much longer chapters set in the past, until everything converges at the end, and layer upon layer of deception is revealed, with surprises right down to almost the last page.<br /><br />Author Chris Pavone paints a detailed, rich, and compelling portrait of Northern Europe and what life is like for transplanted Americans. He centers most of the novel in the tiny country of Luxembourg, known, to the extent that's it known, as a banking and commercial center. (I am myself of first generation Luxembourg descent and so found the author's descriptions and cultural understandings to be deep and fascinating.)<br /><br />Note that I call this novel a psychological mystery. I use term \\"mystery\\" deliberately because the novel is billed as a thriller. It is not a thriller, at least not in the common usage of the term. There is some action, but it's very limited indeed. Most of the book is made up of relations of what Kate is thinking, while she takes what can only be called incremental actions to move the plot forward. There's a lot here about expat moms meeting for coffee, picking up kids at school, and going on extended weekend getaways to somewhere else in (usually) Northern Europe. This is all fine, but it doesn't make for a \\"thriller\\" per se, and it makes the first two-thirds or so of the book drag substantially. I found myself reading the book bits at a time, because frankly it was tedious to read it for an extended period.<br /><br />Still, I don't want to go too far down that road, because the prose is excellent, and the character of Kate equally so. Dexter's character could have used more detailing; Bill and Julia are what they need to be, neither more nor less. The plotting is solid with few obvious holes, a real accomplishment for such a complex work. The descriptions of how the CIA Clandestine Service works are believable enough and don't fall into caricature or exaggeration as too often happens.<br /><br />This is a good debut novel and certainly worth reading, though could have been tighter and better paced. I give it four stars for its many excellent qualities.read it for an extended period.  Still, I don't want to go too far down that road, because the prose is excellent, and the character of Kate equally so. Dexter's character could have used more detailing; Bill and Julia are what they need to be, neither more nor less. The plotting is solid with few obvious holes, a real accomplishment for such a complex work. The descriptions of how the CIA Clandestine Service works are believable enough and don't fall into caricature or exaggeration as too often happens.  This is a good debut novel and certainly worth reading, though could have been tighter and better paced. I give it four stars for its many excellent qualities.

When Halliday, the elderly creator of OASIS dies, he offers his fortune to the player who can solve a series of intricate puzzles and complete a grand quest. 0765322889|3.0|There is a profound intelligence behind this series. It's creators have invested thought, imagination and detail into it and have a fantastic world to explore. I realize the book is supposed to make things dense and obscure. That's fine, I love to work for it, like Umberto Eco does. However some sentences were so obscure I was wondering if he was being deliberately vague. Then I realized the editor was rubbish.  (2) Stop making us guess. After we've been talking about T'lan Imass and Jaghut indirectly for a while, I get them confused. Please explain what these races are. Please explain what a Warren is. An Ascendant? Just a quick definition, nothing deep. Just a sentence? Please?  (3) Does everyone have to be an asshat? I'm fine with everyone having flaws - I don't ask my characters to be Christlike - but if terrible people do terrible things to one another, I'm not really worried about that. Who am I supposed to root for?  (4) Unrealistic Timeline. There are characters who are 300,000 years old who don't show much apparent wisdom. Either allow the characters to grow a bit, or shorten the timeline to something that doesn't make us scoff at how it can't work. Yeah, I get that this is fantasy and we should have imagination, but isn't 300,000 years a bit too long to not learn a darn thing? Mortals work side by side with 300,000 year old beings. This isn't rational and I can't get into it because it's unrealistic. I can do 10,000, but when it gets to 300,000, then why not call someone 1 million years old?  All of the above can be put into the 'different' category, thereby concerning personal taste. But the editor has been too much of a 'light touch' and hasn't worked to discipline their writer. The quality would go up if the work was more approachable. There is a difference between a well-constructed work and one that is hard to read. This is the latter.  However, every time I finish a book, I want to read the next one because I want to learn more about the world. It doesn't feel that the series is building to a particular climax, and that's fine. 0525421033|3.0|If I Stay Critique   If I Stay, by Gayle Forman is a novel about a considerably perfect young seventeen-year-old girl Mia, stuck in a ghostly state after being in a car accident. Her parents both died instantly at the scene, but her body and her younger brother Teddy, were both rushed to the nearest hospital. In this ghostly state, Mia has to choose between life and death and she uses flashbacks/ memories to help sway her decision. Based on all of the characters that come to visit her she one by one remembers her favorite memories of each one, helping her to make the decision.    Gayle Forman did a good job because she wrote about an intriguing topic that is not necessarily found in many books. It is a good, and I would recommend it, because it is a type of book that keeps the reader hanging on and always wanting to read more. It has a lot of suspense. In the book Mia, who is very close to death watches over her body in a ghostly state of being after she was nearly killed in a car accident along with her parents. Through flashbacks of memories Mia tells the readers her different stories of each character that comes to visit the hospital and why she choose to stay alive for them. Reflecting on each memory helps her to sway her decision at the end of the book. In my opinion this is a good way to design a book because it is not just straightforward text to read, it switches it up making it easier to read. Mia being in the state that she is for most of the book makes the story interesting and fun to read because it is not a type of state of being any person would be in after a crash. She makes it relatable and intriguing to see how each character is important to her. The way Forman designed the book helps readers stay engaged and wanting to read more making it a good book to read.   On the other hand the book is very repetitive making it somewhat boring to read. Mia, the main character, tells the story through flash backs and than narrates what is going on in the hospital, switching off back and forth.  Also having no set chapters makes it have a never ending/ concluding point. For some readers like me, they would find that rather boring because the story was just switching off between hospital scenes and flashbacks, there were no other events going on. This makes it somewhat hard for the reader to stay focused to the text as well.   Besides being somewhat boring, this best seller is a good read, and a book that I recommend to readers who like ghost stories with a lot of suspension and detail in description.

The philosophical part of the endeavor is meant to make one awe-struck. 0307888908|3.0|The cofounder and chairman of LinkedIn, Reid Hoffman, has written a book on how to present yourself in the modern job and career marketplace.  Turns out, surprise!, it's all about building and working your professional network, such as LinkedIn best lets you do.  Who would have thought?  There is some good advice, and some homework as well.  Although it's not necessarily blindingly original advice, the authors pause at the end of each chapter to direct the reader to take stock of himself and his career position.  List your assets, hard and soft; what makes you better than anybody else at what you do?  Is there someone you know who can help you play this up, multiply this benefit, help you in some way?  Vice versa?  Make a list of your fears, doubts, insecurities regarding your work and career, then get with three people over coffee to who has pivoted from one career trajectory to another.  how did they do it?  And so on.  The point is that no career is for ever; you have to constantly reevaluate your strengths, weaknesses, and always be looking for what may be your next move, fully cognizant of the fact that your next career may look very very little like your current one.  There is quite a bit on preparing to pivot to a new life, and getting over the fear factor (or comfort zone) of what you are doing now.  It's the new way things are - get over it and get with it, are the author's points.  And in the process of doing this, building and nurturing a support professional network is vital.  The authors don't really help you set one up per se, but encourage the reader to constantly be talking to other people, asking people for help, and being in their face in a variety of ways.  As career advice, there is nothing wrong with it.  Is it new enough of original enough for you to buy this book?  That is a question each customer will have to ask themselves.

0618918248|1.0|What to say about Dawkins's book?<br /><br />Here are some quick observations:<br /><br />* One misconception he has is that religious people are always exclusively religious. But Thomas Aquinas explained, \\"For some of these do not belong to religion ...  such as rubbing one's beard, lifting a stick from the ground and so forth\\" ([[ASIN:0870610635 The Summa Theologica of St. Thomas Aquinas (Five Volumes)]] II-II.186.5)<br /><br />* Another concern is consistency. He critiques the Drake Equation (70-71), but uses a similar line of reasoning supporting the anthropic principle (137-139).<br /><br />* Another instance is his sharp criticism of the God of the Gaps theory, but look at what replaces it with:<br /><br />\\"Natural selection  ... needs some luck to get started, and the 'billions of planets' anthropic principle grants it that luck. Maybe a few later gaps in the evolutionary story also need major infusions of luck, with anthropic justification.\\" (169).<br /><br />In his section on \\"anting,\\" he avers:<br /><br />\\"Nobody knows for sure what the benefit of anting is: perhaps some kind of hygiene, cleansing the feathers of parasites. My point is that uncertainty as to the purpose doesn't--nor should it--stop Darwinians from believing, with great confidence, that anting must be good for something.\\" (164)<br /><br />This is just an ad futurum  cross-dressing as a \\"Darwin of the gaps\\" explanation. Or is it a \\"luck of the gaps\\"?<br /><br />Here are some comments on Chapter 3.<br /><br />As to Aquinas's Five Ways, Dawkins observes, \\"The first three are just different ways of saying the same thing ...\\" (77). But this is nothing new. In fact,  Peter Kreeft one-ups him: \\"These five ways are essentially one way: the 'cosmological argument'\\" ([[ASIN:0898704383 A Shorter Summa: The Essential Philosophical Passages of Saint Thomas Aquinas' Summa Theologica]], 55n19).*<br /><br />Simplistically, asserts that they \\"don't prove anything, and are easily ... exposed as vacuous.\\" (77).<br /><br />Of course they don't prove a thing. Kreeft (who's work should be seriously studied for anyone who wants an honest Christian perspective) he explains that \\"These five are not the proofs themselves, but ways, i.e. indications or summaries of proof. The proofs themselves are elsewhere worked out in much greater detail; e. g. in the Summa Contra Gentiles the first way takes thirty-one paragraphs (Bk 1, chap. 13); here, it takes only one\\" (A Shorter Summa, 55n19).<br /><br />Moreover, these arguments are \\"pre-evenagelism\\" ([[ASIN:084235588X How Now Shall We Live?]], 29-31 ), ideas that create the climate for belief.<br /><br />So much for Dawkins in-depth analysis. (By the way, Kreeft's book costs $10.25 new. Dawkins--drop me a line and I'll send you a copy, gratis!)<br /><br />Dawkins's next step is to criticize \\"the entirely unwarranted assumption that God himself is immune to regress\\" (77). This is a favorite chestnut of his: rhetorically asking \\"who designed the designer\\" (121, 158), and completely ignoring the Christians' response: there is, after all, a book called [[ASIN:0310247101 Who Made God?: And Answers to Over 100 Other Tough Questions of Faith]].<br /><br />He also completely ignores Mormonism's insights on this matter ([[ASIN:0842523219 The Truth, the Way, the Life: An Elementary Treatise on Theology (Byu Studies Monographs)]], Chapter 7; [[ASIN:087579243X Teachings of the Prophet Joseph Smith]], 373).<br /><br />Next, Dawkis changes the subject and begins a discussion on refuting God's omniscience and omnipotence. And he proves this with Karen Owens's doggerel. Case closed.<br /><br />Getting off his professorial tangent, Dawkins then addresses the infinite regress issue But he goes from splitting hairs to splitting atoms. How on earth splitting gold to a lonely atom disproves God's existence (or why atoms are good analogies for God) is never explained. But never mind.<br /><br />He concludes: \\"The atom provides a natural terminator to ... regress It is by no means clear that God provides a natural terminator to the regress of Aquinas\\" Why not? No reason is given. \\"That's putting it mildly, as we shall see later. Let's move down Aquinas' [sic] list.\\"(78)<br /><br />That's his tactic: he makes assertion, then, instead of a fish (or even a stone), he gives us a red herring, then moves on.<br /><br />Due to space, let me move on to his discussion on Anselm. Again, Dawkins completely ignores Aquinas' criticism of Anselm's ideas (A Shorter Summa, 50n8). Then, citing one of his favorite philosophers (and, along with his Warden and Thomas Jefferson, all man-crushes), Dawkins is shocked that Betrand Russell found the argument compelling.<br /><br />I guess this is a selective man-crush.<br /><br />And he says, \\"I suspect that he was an exaggeratedly fair-minded atheist [on Dawkins's part, a Freudian slip], over eager to be disillusioned if logic seemed to require it\\" (82). Considering how Dawkins throws (when convenient) Russel and Stephen Jay Gould under the bus (54-61), the McGarth's point is taken. Dawkins is an atheistic fundamentalist ([[ASIN:0830837213 The Dawkins Delusion?: Atheist Fundamentalism and the Denial of the Divine (Veritas Books)]]).<br /><br />Not doing his own research, Dawkins tells (but does not show) that \\"the trick card up Anselm's sleeve [was] his slippery assumption that 'existence' is more 'perfect' than non-existence\\" (82).<br /><br />So he is not sure than an existing Richard Dawkins is better or more \\"perfect\\" than a non-exitsting Richard Dawkins?<br /><br />Carrying on , Dawkins cites the parody \\"Over Three Hundred Proofs of God's Existence\\" (85). For example, #37 is \\"If things had been different, then things would be different. That would be bad. Therefore God exists\\" (85)<br /><br />Well, along those profound intellectual plumblines, I say \\"Thomas Jefferson said, 'A professorship of theology should have no place in our institution,' (75) therefore God does not exist.\\" \\"Dawkins thought he heard a ghost but it was really the wind blowing through a keyhole (90). Therefore God does not exist.\\"<br /><br />Get the point?<br /><br />People may criticize my review for being sarcastic. So it is. But please remember that Dawkins takes this tack, \\"Ridicule is the only weapon which can be used against unintelligible propositions\\" (34). And, considering that the book is dedicated to Douglas Adams, I hope he appreciates the humor.<br /><br />\\"Nietzsche had some natural talent for sarcasm: he could sneer, though he could not laugh; but there is always something bodiless and without weight in his satire, simply because it has not any mass of common morality behind it.\\"<br />__________________<br /><br />* I guess I should have said two-ups Dawkins, who treats the last two ways separately.n Aquinas' [sic] list.\\"(78)  That's his tactic: he makes assertion, then, instead of a fish (or even a stone), he gives us a red herring, then moves on. B004J4WKTW|5.0|The Night Circus is about imagery, rhythmic writing, and emotional scenes. For me, this is what I must have in a great novel. And as a debut? Wow. It is told in rotating third with some 2nd person thrown in. At one point I thought that the second person mini-scenes were superfluous, but after reading the entire story, they add a certain shape that makes this novel unique. What's great about this story is that it is literature without trying to prove itself in a flurry of details. It just IS. You know what it's like? Take the imagination of Alice and Wonderland and marry it to the emotion of The Time Travelers Wife.  When I say flawed, what I really mean is a willingness by the author to abandon everyone else's notion of what a book should be and just let her story present itself with moles and all. But I've always thought natural beauty is far better than the majority of \\"perfect,\\" contrived MFA-ish prose. It's nice to see someone write a story who hasn't been taught what great writing should look like. Morgenstern has found this alchemical key within herself.

1451648537|4.0|Isaacson writes well, but two things really make the book worth reading: (1) it is about Steve Jobs, a man who was many things but never boring; and (2) unlike essentially everyone else writing about the topic, he had authorized access to Jobs and his associates. A lot of Isaacson's narrative, especially about anything before Jobs's second tenure at Apple, is not groundbreaking, and some of his analysis of the last decade at Apple is flawed by his lack of technical expertise. (John Siracusa, an apple tech blogger, has argued that Steve Jobs essentially chose the wrong biographer, just as he was wrong in his choice of John Sculley as Apple CEO.) Nonetheless, to have the whole arc of Jobs's life and accomplishments brought together in one accessible package is a significant achievement. This will be the standard narrative for one of the true giants of the computer/tech revolution.<br /><br />I should add that like many other reviewers I was surprised--and a bit disappointed--by how little regard Jobs seems to have had for the the feelings of others throughout his career. He could be nasty and petty for no great reason. I was glad that Isaacson was honest about this, though, and that he didn't view his work as a mere hagiography. He doesn't fall into the trap of giving us a one-dimensional hero who's simply too good to be true. There's plenty of things to admire in Jobs's achievement, but his personal behavior isn't necessarily one of them.

For those unfamiliar with the series, I would advise watching Season 1 first as it provides a lot of background information on many of the characters, as well as continuity.<br /><br />The usually robust and confident Earl of Grantham(I love Hugh Bonneville's portrayal of the benevolent Earl) feels somewhat out of place this season as he yearns to be of use to his country, yet all he gets is an honorary rank in the military, and is not considered for active duty due to his age.  Cora, Countess Grantham (Elizabeth McGovern) gets so caught up with the rehabilitation hospital set up in a wing of Downton that she neglects her husband which has repercussions for the Grantham's marriage. Lady Mary (Michelle Dockery) is melancholic over her failed romance with Matthew Crawley and is resigned to the prospect of a marriage of convenience with another; Lady Edith (Laura Carmichael) takes up driving and finds a sense of purpose in an otherwise uneventful life; and, lovely Lady Sybil (Jessica Brown-Findlay) with her ideals and radical views trains as a nurse.<br /><br />The staff of Downton undergo changes of their own, and experience both ups and downs. Brendan Coyle who plays the Earl's faithful valet, John Bates, experiences some serious challenges when his estranged wife, the odious Vera (Maria Kennedy) turns up. The battle scenes are credibly done, evoking all the fear and horror of the war. But I admit I was more interested in the machinations back home at Downton, and watching familiar and new characters resort to manipulation, scheming, and intrigues. Dowager Countess Grantham is one of my favorite characters, and Dame Maggie Smith's performance is excellent as always. 145162445X|5.0|This book is a tour de force in cosmology and where it stands today. Lawrence Krauss gives us the physicists definition of \\"nothing\\" and shows that it is in fact \\"something\\". In fact \\"nothing\\" using a philosopher's definition doesn't exist. Even if space and time, all matter including every last atom were removed, we would have a quantum field which is seething with subatomic particles popping in and out of existence.<br /><br />In layman's terms Krauss shows us the frightening reality we find ourselves in. A flat universe that will eventually expand or stretch until eventually every distant galaxy will be forever gone to us, even by the biggest telescopes we will be a \\"lone\\" galaxy. Any cosmologists living then will not have the same picture of the universe that we have today as the last of the galaxies stretch beyond our sights faster than the speed of light. B002G1YPH0|5.0|This is a fantastic product, and a must-have for any PC builder or for anyone who wants to keep their CPU super cold.  Keeping your PC cool is critical for extending the life of the CPU and also permits generous overclocking should you be so inclined.  Pros  -Comes with thermal paste (why spend an extra $12?)  -Incredibly durable product  -Very high quality  -The gold-standard in air cooling  -Cheap  Cons  -Installation can be a bit tricky, especially for a first timer.  It took several Youtube clips before I finally got my head around how this is supposed to be installed.  I highly recommend you avoid using the instruction booklet and just watch a couple different Youtube videos before you attempt installation.  -The cooler is monstrous.  Please make sure your case will contain this cooler.  I'm using the Challenger U3 gaming case, and it fits perfectly.  So what did this cooler do for me?  I paired this with a i5-2500K.  Here are my temps so far:  Idle:  Generally around 29C.  It will dip to 26C (at night).  Light windows activity:  Generally around 30 - 34C.  Gaming:  I ran Starcraft 2 on Extreme settings, in a 4x4 map, and my max temp was 42C.  Highest temp so far:  46C --> Occurred during the Windows Experience Index.  These temps are amazing -- and totally make this a MUST BUY!  Applying the thermal paste:  You will hear a lot of different methods to apply the thermal paste.  In the end, it probably doesn't matter too much.  However, here is what I did and I'm very happy with my results.  First, I put a pea-size drop on the CPU and spread it around (with my finger in a plastic bag, of course!!) until the entire CPU was covered with a thin layer on top.  I then put the cooler on top.  Unfortunately, my mobo was dead and I had it RMA'd, so I had to remove the cooler and uninstall the CPU.  I left the paste on the CPU and cooler, though.  However, when my new mobo arrived, I re-applied the thermal paste using the same technique as before.  I once again set the cooler on top of the CPU.  Unfortunately, I had to remove the cooler yet again, at which time I repeated the entire process again.  Long story short - I applied a thin layer of thermal paste 3x before I finally was finished installing the cooler.  The results speak for themselves.  Thus, if you have time, I'd say apply and reapply the paste a few times, spreading a thin layer on top each time.  Worked for me!

B002GJT4YQ|4.0|Harry Potter and the Chamber of Secrets<br />Ultimate Edition Blu-Ray<br /><br />Year:  2002<br />Rating:  PG<br />Runtime:  2 HRS 41 MIN (Theatrical), 2 HRS, 54 MIN (Extended)<br /><br />Directed:  Chris Columbus<br />Starring:  Daniel Radcliffe, Emma Watson, Rupert Grint, Robbie Coltrane, Richard Harris, Maggie Smith, Alan Rickman, and Kenneth Branagh<br /><br />Film Rating:  3 out of 5 stars<br />Video Rating:  4 out of 5 stars<br />Audio Rating:  4 out of 5 stars<br />Extras:  4 out of 5 stars<br />Overall Rating:  4 out of 5 stars<br /><br />The Film<br /><br />As much as I hated Harry Potter and the Sorcerer's Stone upon my first viewing I hated it's follow-up even more.  In the year following my first viewing of Sorcerer's Stone I hadn't bothered to re-watch the film so I went into the sequel with very little recollection of the events of that film.  To say I felt lost was an understatement, I didn't understand how the events in the Sorcerer's Stone tied to the Chamber of Secrets and by the end of this film I swore to never watch another Harry Potter film again.<br /><br />Harry Potter and the Chamber of Secrets picks up during Harry's summer holiday from Hogwarts.  His neglectful Aunt and Uncle are continuing their quest to try to stifle Harry's magical ability and force him to live a normal life.  Then one night Harry is visited by a strange creature named Dobby (who is a House Elf) who informs Harry he must not return to school or terrible things will happen at Hogwarts.  Harry of course disregards this warning from Doobie (I find it a lot more fun to call him Doobie rather than Dobby, I'm not sure why) and heads back to school anyways.  Of course, terrible things do begin happen at Hogwarts, most of which have something to do with the \\"Chamber of Secrets\\". Shockingly enough, our hero and his faithful friends must solve the mystery and of course, save the day.  There's also more Quiddich, a new Defense Against the Dark Arts teacher, and a few new characters are introduced that will play more integral parts in the series later on.<br /><br />In my review of Harry Potter and Sorcerer's Stone I went into detail about several issues that I thought hindered the film from being great.  Those issues included the film's director Chris Columbus, the child acting being less than amazing, and some shoddy CGI work by 2001 standards.  Chamber of Secrets improves on two out of three of those issues.  Chris Columbus is still in the director's chair for this installment and unfortunately not much has changed concerning his handling of the film.  Chamber of Secrets is bland and lacks the visual flare you'd expect to see from a film on this scale.  Far too often dramatic scenes are followed up with scenes that are filled with juvenile humor that robs the film of any real sense of danger or foreboding.  CGI work is improved on and doesn't show it's age quite as much as Sorcerer's Stone.  If you watch Sorcerer's Stone and Chamber of Secrets back-to-back you'll see a clear difference in the quality of the CGI, a great example comes in the form of the Quiddich match.  The first film's match looked pretty damn lousy whereas Chamber of Secrets looks somewhat decent by comparison. Where the film does clearly outclass its predecessor is in the children's performances.  While they're are still some issues with some of the youth cast, most make at least moderate improvements.  Emma Watson finally learned that she didn't have to over enunciate every other word in the script which made her less annoying over the course of a 2 1/2+ hour runtime.  Overall, most of the kids seem to feel a lot more comfortable in this film and that's important when they're the focus of the film.<br /><br />The \\"big\\" name addition to the cast is Kenneth Branagh (who recently directed Thor) as the new Defense Against the Dark Arts teacher Gilderoy Lockhart.  He replaced Hugh Grant who dropped out due to scheduling conflicts and I'm glad he did because Branagh's performance is often scene stealing and is one of highlights of the film.  Chamber of Secrets also introduces us to a few new adult characters that we'll see a lot more of in future films.  Mark Williams plays the Weasely's muggle obsessed father Arthur and Jason Isaacs plays Lucius Malfoy, the smarmy father of Harry Potter's school rival Draco Malfoy.  Throughout the books and to a lesser extent the films, fatherhood is an important underlying issue and these two characters represent the polar opposites on how to be a dad.  I'd be remiss not to mention the character of Doobie (I know it's Dobby but again, I like Doobie better) who I absolutely despised during my initial viewing of Chamber of Secrets.  After reading the books my feeling changed a bit for the character so if you're anything like me you might find yourself saying \\"@#$% this movie, Doobie pisses me off.\\"  Go ahead and take a deep breath and say to yourself, \\"I won't see this character again for quite a few movies\\" and move on.  He can be a bit much to handle but if you read the books your feeling for Doobie might change some and you might even find yourself liking the little bastard.<br /><br />Overall, Harry Potter and the Chamber of Secrets is a better film than Sorcerer's Stone.  It's not leaps and bounds better but I think to those that are still Potter novices this film will be more accessible to them since they've already been exposed to the world of Harry Potter.  As with the first film, Chamber of Secrets stumbles some thanks mostly to it's director Chris Columbus and his wishy-washy direction and pacing.  Hardcore fans will argue that this is the purest film in the franchise due to how closely it follows the novel but what works on the page doesn't always translate well to the screen, so I wish the film would have deviated slightly from the book to make a involving film.<br /><br />The Blu-Ray<br /><br />Harry Potter and the Chamber of Secret's Ultimate Edition Blu-ray comes in an almost identical case as Sorcerer's Stone.  The main difference between to two aside from the artwork is the color of the cases and the numbering.  Sorcerer's Stone is blue and is labeled Year One and Chamber of Secrets is red and is labeled Year Two.  Inside our sturdy cardboard slipcover you'll find both the theatrical and extended cuts of the film, a second Blu-ray loaded with special features, a standard-def (SD) DVD of special features, a digital copy of the film (theatrical cut only), a collectible book, and two character cards (Rubeus Hagrid & Severus Snape)<br /><br />Just as in the case of Sorcerer's Stone, Chamber of Secrets features both the theatrical and extended cut of the film.  The extended cut is approximately 13 minutes longer than its theatrical counterpart bringing the film dangerously close to three hours long.  Most of the added footage isn't that exciting and amount to a few small moments of interest or are just extensions of existing scenes.  Book purists will be pleased that these moments have been added back in but if you decide to watch the theatrical cut you won't be missing much.<br /><br />Chamber of Secrets features the same Hi-Def transfer used for the previous Blu-ray and HD-DVD.  Only a year separates the first and second films but Chamber of Secrets has a better looking transfer than the first film.  Colors pop more, black levels are inkier, and detail is improved and more consistent.  Grain level is always appropriate and thankfully I didn't notice any use of DNR (Digital Noise Reduction) which if applied heavily can make people look like plastic or appear waxy.<br /><br />Featuring a DTS-HD Master Audio 6.1 track for the theatrical cut of the film and a DTS-HD Master Audio 5.1 track for the extended cut Harry Potter sounds great no matter what version you watch. Dialogue is always crystal clear even when the score or on-screen action picks up.  Large chunks of this film are dialogue driven but when the action kicks into gear you'll notice impressive bass and good activity from the surround speakers.  One scene features hundreds of spiders crawling down from the surrounding trees to attack Harry and Ron and it's an extremely well done moment that made me check over my shoulder to make sure that no creepy-crawlers weren't about to pounce on me either.<br /><br />Disc one only offers up one special feature which is the PiP (Picture in Picture) commentary track with director Chris Columbus.  Some of what he has to say is interesting and the visual aids are mildly entertaining but there are long gaps in between segments and when the movie is almost three hours long that gets a little tiresome especially if you just watched the film all the way through.<br /><br />As with the previous Potter Ultimate Edition, the Creating the World of Harry Potter documentary is the premiere special feature in this set.  Part II:  Characters is an 80 minute long installment covering every character of mention in the Harry Potter series, not just those in Chamber of Secrets.  There's a good mix of interviews both new and old that delivers some great insight into not only how the characters have grown but how the actors have as well.  The documentary is filled with lots of behind the scenes footage and candid interviews from both the youth and adult cast.  This documentary series should be reason enough for some die hard fans invest in Ultimate Edition sets, they are right up there with the best documentaries I've seen offered on any Blu-ray or DVD set.  Next up is 12 minutes worth of screen tests of Daniel Radcliffe, Emma Watson, and Rupert Grint which are presented in HD.  I'm surprised these were not included on the Sorcerer's Stone set but because of the \\"Characters\\" documentary, they thought the screen tests were a good companion piece.  Also included in HD are a selection of deleted scenes, most of which are included in the Extended Cut, total runtime of the deleted scenes is 17 minutes.  The last special features on the disc is the teaser (SD), trailer (HD), and 17 TV spots all presented in (SD)... as usual, if it's not in HD I hardly considerate worth watching which brings me to disc three.  The third disc in our set is full of non-HD extras that have been ported over from the old DVD releases and therefore I have once again avoided filling my Blu-ray player with such filth.<br /><br />Harry Potter and the Chamber of Secrets is a stronger entry than the Sorcerer's Stone but the franchise still hasn't hit its stride yet.  The kid actors have improved and the CGI is holds up a lot better but the film still suffers from Chris Columbus' overly family friendly style of film making.  If you made it through Sorcerer's Stone and are still interested in knowing what happens next to Harry and his friends then I think you'll enjoy Chamber of Secrets.  If you find yourself struggling through keep in mind that Harry Potter and the Prisoner of Azkaban is coming up next and that's where the film series takes huge leaps forward.<br /><br />Conclusion<br /><br />I highly recommend Harry Potter and the Chamber of Secrets especially if you're a Potter fanatic.  If you're not interested in the wonderful Creating the World of Harry Potter documentaries or are a little more price consensus you can always pick up the single disc Blu-ray for about $10 and still get great video and audio minus the extended cut and documentary.  Either way you've got less than six weeks till Deathly Hallows Part 2 is released to get up to speed... 1599218011|5.0|Kris Carr's Crazy Sexy Diet is more a way of life than a diet.  Carr is an inspiration: many people who've gotten her diagnosis, or something similar, are all too willing to take meds and give up.  I very much appreciate the lesson she teaches, that you can fix yourself from the inside out.  A lot of the things that we've been taught about food are myths and products of subsidies and other deals.  Eating right and being healthy is not hard if you want it.  If you can get over your addictions to sugar and the like, you can change your life.  She makes sense.  As for me, I can tell you that while I was eating healthfully as Carr teaches, I felt great.  Just being and eating clean opened up sources of energy I never knew I had.  Even my attitude was better.  I have since relapsed with my addiction to sugar, and I have to reread this book and snap out of that.  These kinds of books are what you make them.  It is important to note here that not all of the dietary restrictions that Carr prescribes will work for everyone, and she advocates doing what works for you.  The juicing portion of the book is invaluable.  The vitamins and minerals in fresh vegetables and fruits are what moves your system.  I was a little disappointed that there were not more recipes, but Carr has just come out with a new book devoted specifically to juicing and blending.  An alkaline diet is also important.  Think about it: if you can feel the acidity in your body in your chest every night after dinner, say, best to avoid those foods, isn't it?  Germs and viruses thrive in alkaline conditions.  If you could change just a few things in your diet and rid yourself of the flu, bronchitis, yeast infections, bacterial infections, etc., wouldn't you?  I've eliminated certain things in my diet and I've stuck with it, but I'm not going to tell you I don;'t still crave them.  Take soda, for example.  I was a big diet soda drinker, since childhood all the way up until a year or so ago. 1594203334|4.0|Having actually READ this book, I saw it not as a treatise on French parenting, but rather the author's investigation of French cultural traditions underlying their parenting style. As an American, Druckerman contrasts the foibles of raising her own rapidly growing family in Paris against her observations of the calm and disciplined French approach. Her approach is witty, engaging and funny! She's not overly academic, more self-deprecating in fact, as she investigates how French families make raising children look so effortless. So armed with a degree in International Affairs from Columbia University and sound journalistic credentials (Wall Street Journal, New York Times) Druckerman begins her inquiry deep into French culture to discover the basis of their methods. Her investigation forms the crux of the book recounted in an amusing narrative style.  If you're interested in cultural studies, I recommend the book for this alone; because as an experienced journalist, Druckerman carefully unpeels the layers of the French cultural \\"mystique\\" through interviews with noted experts and by exploring documented research. She also conducts candid discussions with friends and associates to uncover how cultural traditions are applied in their own families. She distills her findings into several maxims on French parenting, culture and life...and they're good!  While she's not too in your face with her new found wisdom (as some reviewers have implied), she does poke fun at the laissez-faire American parenting style beset by screaming, unruly toddlers; and that may offend some! In all fairness to Americans though, our culture is predicated on the self-made individualist who triumphs over adversity. And while American's overindulgent parenting has gone TOO far in some respects, cultivating a certain degree of competitiveness is imperative for survival here; Druckerman criticizes Americans without underscoring that cultural difference. But in the end, her story should cause some parents to check their own ideas with a French \\"le pause\\" and attempt to raise children that are independent and cultivated too. As a parent myself, I couldn't agree with her more!

B00004R7TP|5.0|Hounds of Love is a haunting experience.  You see I was 4 years old when this album was released in 1985.  The music within perfectly captures the vibes and memories of the mid 80's.  It takes me back to those golden preschool years and, even though I've never actually heard Hounds of Love back then, the atmosphere is so spiritual that it's like the music was there with me the whole time.<br /><br />\\"Hello Earth\\" is a back and forth exchange between two different types of religious chants.  You have Kate Bush singing the female chants, and the male voices providing the role of those particular chants.  The results are that the atmosphere is extraordinarily impactful and definitely an example of art-rock utilized to its full potential.  \\"Running Up That Hill (A Deal with God)\\" continues with the hazy, memorable vibes of the mid 80's with vocals tucked comfortably in the background while a dance beat keeps the rhythm flowing.  It's oddly spiritual and I honestly don't even *want* the song to speed up.  It would totally ruin the atmosphere.  The way it is, is just fine.  Madonna tried to write a song this good back in the day but... to tell you the truth, I really don't wanna bash Madonna or compare her magnificent work to Kate Bush.  Two different artists.<br /><br />\\"Cloudbusting\\" has string arrangements that are totally fitting.  The entire song is pleasantly repetitive, and that's how it SHOULD be.  Kate's vocals grow on you dramatically too- they seem forgettable at first and then, BAM, suddenly you're falling in love with them.  There's definitely a religious vibe running through this entire album and it's not necessarily Kate's voice *alone* giving it such an overwhelming effect- it's actually the repetitive nature of the material.  It's hard to explain but it works.  This album was released at just the right time in history.  Man, this album just gets better.  I swear it does!!  \\"The Big Sky\\" is based around an irresistible beat.  I refuse to resist!  I must dance the night away!  Dance the night away... creatively and spiritually. Well this album is going to haunt me for another reason after being hit with some terrible news tonight.  My basset hound of 14 years might be put to sleep tomorrow because his health has fallen apart suddenly.  You see what I DIDN'T mention when I said this album is \\"haunting\\" is that in 1986 my mom developed cancer and that completely changed my life.  Despite not being familiar with this album back in 1986, the atmosphere contains every unusual, and yes, *haunting* nook and cranny that takes me back to those dreaded days.  There's just something about Kate's heavenly voice, the eerie and \\"fluffy\\" arrangements, and the time period that makes it hard for me to replay it.  With this tragic news of my dog... and considering the album is called Hounds of Love... yikes!  I might never play the album again.  Still, don't let me personal situation interfere with the enjoyment *you* will probably get out of the album.  I'd never expect my personal life to change somebody elses point of view.It would be selfish on my part to expect that.  You have your own memories and your own life, so I'd never want to lay my set of horrendous circumstances on another person.  None of this changes the 5-star excellent rating this album most certainly deserves, of course.  It's just unfortunate this album has that \\"bad news\\" vibe that's impossible for me to shake off, and now the bad news relates to TWO personal circumstances, ugh.<br /><br />Update- March 15, 2012.  Well he was just put to sleep and I've been spending the last couple hours crying.  What a crappy day.  Again though, don't let my personal feelings affect how you perceive Hounds of Love.  It's a fantastic album that I'm apparently destined to never listen to given the two horrible events I mention above.

Hal Moore just turned 90 two days ago, so this is a good time to review this incredible book.<br /><br />At the time of the battle of Ia Drang, which is the focus of \\"We Were Soldiers Once... And Young\\", the airborne cavalry was an unproven experiment in a new kind of warfare. Where helicopters would take the place of horses to quickly deploy the soldiers of the 7th Calvary. Hal Moore (then Lt. Col.), a devout Christian, a father and a loving husband found himself at the tip of the spear, as his battalion (450 men) was surrounded by upwards of 2000 North Vietnamese soldiers. So he was both greatly outnumbered and without his biggest advantage, which was the ability to move faster then the enemy.<br /><br />All of which makes this book less about the fighting and tactics of the battle, although that's here too in heart-wrenching detail, and more about the leadership, courage, and humility of Moore. Ultimately, the is a story about strength found through faith, sacrifice and love for one's brothers in arms. A real life example of a universal theme (to be sure).<br /><br />Joseph Galloway, like the old-school journalist he is, loves to avoid the limelight. It's a shame, because his accomplishments and experiences are no less impressive then Moore's. This book would not be anywhere near as powerful if Galloway had not been there with the men of the 1st Battalion during this battle.<br /><br />This book will leave you crying and thankful for your blessings. 1439192812|4.0|After finishing this book, I told my wife we're finally ready to have kids, now that this book is around.  Written at a junior high level, this was Richard Dawkins' attempt to write a book of general science that answers a lot of common questions from kids. Questions like \\"How do we know how far away things are in space?\\" or \\"Where did the dinosaurs go?\\"  Love him or hate him, there's no denying that Professor Dawkins is a master of explaining difficult-to-grasp scientific concepts. Though I felt like I understood everything in the book pretty well before going through it, his explanations were all very easy to understand and well-argued.  Each chapter of this book begins with some sort of mythology story, from all over the world. Dawkins focuses on how different peoples and different religions answer questions in various supernatural ways, and how now science can give us the answers that people have been searching for all this time.  This book is not an attack on religion, like his most famous work: The God Delusion. Rather, it's like his less famous, but overall much better The Selfish Gene, or The Greatest Show on Earth.  I recommend this book to everyone over the age of 10. At the VERY LEAST, it will make you think. And you will likely end up with a deeper understanding of fundamental scientific concepts.

Neo chooses truth and soon wakes in a gel-filled pod in a techno-hell future. The rest threatens to stop it permanently. There's plenty of CGI, some it good (the Sentinels) some it horrible (anything with Neo and Smith). The fight scenes are now just a bore. Watching people walk on the ceiling while firing guns doesn't excite me. Watching people slam into wall and making a big hole got old the first hundred times it happened too.<br /><br />You've never seen a film with such unjustifiable delusions of grandeur. B003291CBW|5.0|NPR aired an interview with Christopher Hitchens (not sure of date) where he discussed the writings of George Orwell. I think most people were expecting maybe a short discourse on Orwells most famous work '1984' but Hitchens instead gave props to 'Homage To Catalonia'. And after reading this 'one mans viewpoint' on the Spanish Civil War I find myself in agreement with Hitchens that this book is underrated and unfortunately overshadowed by '1984'. Although It originally did not sell well in England and even today is still rather obscure, the book should be standard reading in Highschool and College; especially in Political Science classes.  Orwells non-fiction 'Homage to Catalonia' should be a standard text for war reporting to any aspiring journalist. In a modest, sometimes witty, and almost modern travel blogger style, Orwell lays out his experience as a foreign soldier fighting with the P.O.U.M against the Franco Fascists and then later his further involvement with the internal political battles between the Communists and Anarchists in Barcelona. The following passage on pages 130-131 is a good example of Orwells style and the strength of this book,  \\"I used to sit on the roof marveling at the folly of it all. From the little windows in the observatory you could see for miles around- vista after vista of tall slender buildings, glass domes and fantastic curly roofs with brilliant green and copper tiles; over to eastward the glittering pale blue sea - the first glimpse of the sea that I had coming to Spain. And the whole huge town of a million people(Barcelona) was locked in a sort of violent inertia, a nightmare of noise without movement. The sunlit streets were quite empty. Nothing was happening except the streaming of bullets from barricades and sand bagged windows.  Not a vehicle was stirring in the streets; here and there along the Ramblas the trams stood motionless where their drivers had jumped out of them when the fighting started. And all the while the devilish noise, echoing from thousands of stone buildings, went on and on, like a tropical rainstorm. Crack, crack, rattle-rattle, roar - sometimes it died away to a few shots, sometimes it quickened to a deafening fusillade, but it never stopped while daylight lasted, and punctually next dawn it started again.\\"  Much of the book is written like the above quoted passage. Orwells clear and descriptive observations make for great reading without the need for the more modern exaggerated styles of writing that try to make every moment a life and death situation as if war is something 24/7 and 365 days a year. Orwell understands that people are human. And even the enemy must sleep, eat, and defecate at some point. The book is without flaw but you might find the constant political party references and alphabet soup of acronyms overwhelming if you don't take notes. P.O.U.M, P.S.U.C., C.N.T., and F.A.I. are just some of the abbreviated party names you will need to remember especially for Chapter 11 when Orwell attempts to sort out for the reader exactly what happened during the fighting in Spain.  This edition had a good introduction by Lionel Trilling which I recommend reading after the book as a supplement to further understanding Orwell and the politics of the Spanish Civil War.

1594203334|5.0|I pre-ordered this book in hardback for our daughter who is expecting our first grandchild in July. Besides the witty and humorous style that makes this an easy read, this book contains very useful information for all parents.  I am a psychologist and had the good fortune to be raised in the 40s/50s timeframe in a stable family environment.  I believe that many (not all) of the parenting insights in this book were in use by thoughtful parents of that period and were attributed to 'common sense' or some such.  Who at that time could have predicted that today most children and their parents no longer eat together during well-established mealtimes, that months and months of baby sleep problems are expected and considered normal, that an entire industry has sprung up to help today's parents push their children faster and faster, that many children eat only what they want to eat, and that today's children seem to need constant stimulation, instant gratification, and are becoming increasingly obese?  Of course, parents will choose their own parenting strategies, and this book will contribute in different ways to each of us.  I believe that if you read this book and pick out some of the \\"universal truths\\" regarding children and their development, you will improve your parenting ability.  One of these truths is to carefully observe and become attuned to the baby and his/her \\"rhythms\\" rather than focusing on your own fears and needs and letting them drive your reactions to the baby.  This is also where 'the pause' comes in - you need to read the book to get what you need on this subject.  Another truth is to teach your child to handle frustration and not always get his/her own way - again you need to read the book about this and apply your own common sense and life circumstances to this area.  The author's approach is to present real-life examples of what we have today versus a better outcome we might desire, and then talk about real parental actions and research that supports achieving those better results.  You are free to take what you wish from this book.  I strongly recommend it to all parents and GRANDPARENTS, because we as grandparents must not undermine the positive parenting of our daughter and son-in-law.

0439023513|3.0|(Minor Spoilers!)  As an extreme fan of the first two books, I was extremely excited for the closure in Collins' final installment in the series. Unfortunately, this book seems to have little relation in style when compared to Catching Fire and the Hunger Games. I enjoyed the book as a whole, but I was somehow extremely disappointed with the way the story unfolded.  The closure in this book is great. The way that Collins weaved Katniss's realization of who she really was in the world of two extremes really created a sense of fulfillment while I was reading. Collins also goes on to discuss the endings and legacies of most if not all past characters of significance. However, the sudden change within Katniss in both personality and character is not only annoying, but it can be EXTREMELY frustrating. At times, the character that Collins creates within Mockingjay resembles little of the Katniss within the first two books. Furthermore, despite the closure of characters, some significant heroes just seemingly drop off the face of the Panem. Collins seems to just disregard their importance in the storyline, at times, and the reader is left empty and confused on \\"what just happened?\\" Not only is the story extremely dark (even for HG standards), but the story is also very poorly paced. It seems as if the majority of the actual plot happens within the last 150 pages of the book. This leads to a horribly rushed conclusion, which amazing, still remains very satisfying if a reader can accept the sudden disappearances of favorite characters.  Overall, Mockingjay is not a bad book. It, however, does not even come close to comparing to the first two books in the trilogy. A reader will either be completely pissed off at the end or satisfied with the conclusiveness of the actual plot. I most definitely recommend this book to those who are fans of the trilogy, but I would like to warn fans of the series that this is NOT like the other two books by any stretch of the imagination. The characters return, but the writing style has changed and the signature pacing that the books are famous for is just non-existent. Just be prepared to be assaulted with a nonstop barrage of emotional turmoil that WILL move you as a reader.

When it came in the mail, I immediately popped it into my car cd player and settled in for some lovely commutes.<br />Cinder doesn't disappoint on any level. The narrator has a solid, pleasant voice and does a stellar job of distinguishing different characters and accents. With an intriguing plot (Cinderella meets Black plague meets desperate future world), I was captivated from start to finish. I frequently found myself sitting in my car after arriving home for \\"just 3 more minutes.\\" When the ten hours of audio finished, I was blown away by the ending. Cinder is the first book of a set and it left me craving more. Throughout the book I kept asking myself the following questions: Would Cinder find happiness? Would Kai figure out what happened in time? What was the cure for the disease? Is the doctor good or bad? Will Cinder save her sister? There are plenty of questions in this book to keep you jumping from start to finish, but the book is so well written that I stayed on the edge of my steering wheel for the entire ride.<br /><br />Marissa Meyer noted on her twitter feed that the next book is not due out until next January and, frankly, I don't know if I can wait that long. Cinder has brought me into this future world and the audiobook held me in it like a child being read a fairytale at night before bed. I found myself thinking about the book during the day and it allowed that escape that few novels do anymore. Cinder is an excellent young adult novel with no foul language, that I heard, and no real adult themes unless you count a cyborg almost short circuiting when in close contact with a handsome prince or an android giggling about picture a human prince in the nude as a real adult theme. I found them charming and like the things that young teenagers giggle about at slumber parties.<br /><br />Cinder is a beautiful, sweet-natured, refreshing change of pace and I appreciate MacMillon Audio for allowing me a free copy to review. B004RFYEEC|3.0|I've been a Beach Boys fan since the early 1980's, and I wanted to share my opinion of the SMiLE Sessions box set.  For the last 30 years or so I've been collecting SMiLE bootlegs: on album, cassette, and compact disc. When I heard that The Beach Boys were finally going to release this material I thought, \\"Yeah, I gotta see this to believe it!\\" So when it finally was released, I was shocked to say the least.  Being unemployed at the moment, I saved my pennies and I saved my dimes and bought a copy as a Christmas present to myself. Mostly because I didn't want someone spending that kind of money on me. Luckily, I found a copy on sale. It was a bargain, but not the best I ever had.  There's a lot of cool stuff inside the box! I really like the 60 page case bound book, the two 7\\" singles w/picture sleeves, and the huge poster of the album artwork. I especially like the reproduction of the original SMiLE booklet! I've never seen an original, so I never knew what it looked like.  I was really disappointed when I found out that SMiLE was issued in a mono mix, with stereo session highlights. I mean come on, it's a 5 CD box set! You'd think they'd make room for a good wide-channel stereo mix. And by good stereo, I mean someone other than Mark Linett mixing it. As I'm sure anyone that's listened to the last couple of Beach Boys' compilation CDs will agree: his \\"stereo\\" mixes leave a lot to be desired! He mixes good mono, but his stereo mixes have that \\"might-as-well-be-mono\\" feel to them.  I'm sure Mr. Linett is a nice guy, he just mixes lousy stereo.  'Wonderful' is the only SMiLE song that I don't have in stereo. I really dig the original version from 1993 Good Vibrations box set and wanted to hear that song in a good stereo mix more than anything. The only one I've heard in stereo is that stupid \\"Rock with me Henry\\" version. And wouldn't you know The Beach Boys would put THAT version on the box set!  After hearing 'Cabin Essence' in stereo for decades, a mono mix will take some getting used to. I do however, like the mono mix of 'Surf's Up,' it's very interesting. I especially like the \\"Are you sleeping?\\" vocal blend.  I was also bummed that no stereo mix of 'Good Vibrations' was included. Dick Bartley plays one on his Saturday night oldies show. I got that on cassette, it sounds incredible! Too bad one wasn't included here.  Some things should have been left off: Brian falls into a piano, the talking horns, Brian falls into a microphone. Yeah, especially the talking horns! I heard that on a bootleg LP in 1982. I didn't like it then and I still don't like it now! But, I really dig 'Teeter Totter Love'! I've palyed that more times than anything else on the box set!  Here's how it should have been released: CD 1- SMiLE in mono with bonus tracks of alternate mono mixes. CD 2- SMiLE in wide-channel stereo with bonus tracks of alternate stereo mixes. CD 3- highlights of various 'G.V.' and 'Heroes And Villains' sessions instead of taking up two CDs! CDs 4 and 5- the remainder of the SMiLE songs. Had they done that, in my opinion, it would have been a far better box set and well worth the money.  Like I wrote earlier: there's a lot of cool things in the box set. But music wise, I can't help but feel I was ripped off.

B0067LY4WG|5.0|Leonard Cohen is not known to spoil his large audience with many new songs / albums. So naturally there are always high hopes and expectations for his rare new releases. After the album \\"Dear Heather\\" from 2004, one might fear that the old voice and perhaps the inspiration was close to exhaustion. There are obviously fine songs on \\"Dear Heather\\", but they are fewer than you are accustomed to from Cohen, and his voice appear worn and slightly weak on several tracks.<br /><br />I was fortunate to experience him live on his recent European tour, so I knew that he in many ways is still an entertainer at the highest level and that his voice if it just presented right is at least as expressive as when he was younger. Over the years he is also known for being very adept at finding the right musicians, and especially the right singers to back him up.<br /><br />On this new album the list of people who have vcontributed is very long - both concerning producers, arrangers, singers and musicians. However, fortunately, they have managed to create a beautiful album that serves as a beautiful whole, and you donever notice that the whole team may be changed from one song to the next.<br /><br />Like all Cohen's later albums it is beautifully produced: Very pleasant sound - never get boring nor irrelevant, however. Cohen's songwriting is still fine, though there hardly many new great classics to find. On several of the songs he has had help on melody side from producer Patrick Leonard and on one from Anjani Thomas.<br /><br />There are no weak tracks - they all appear finished, even though the lyrics on a couple of tracks may seem a little sparse. The theme of the songs is for the most part getting old - looking back at a long life and to embrace death.<br /><br />Of personal favorites, I will mention \\"Going Home\\" - a quiet melodic song with very nice vocal backing by Dana Glover.<br /><br />On the gospel-like and melancholy \\"Show Me\\" Cohen is supported on vocals by his old friend Jennifer Warnes. Perhaps the finest number on the album.<br /><br />On the melodic \\"Come Healing\\" it is again Dana Glover who is responsible for the great harmony vocals.<br /><br />A small handful of songs draw well on the blues. \\"Darkness\\" with his latest tour band in the backing is a fine and tight recording, and it's always nice to listen to Sharon Robinson and the Webb Sisters. Robinson is also present on the less significant \\"Banjo\\". On \\"Lullaby\\" you notice a nice melodic guitar line.<br /><br />The final track \\"Different Sides\\" is a worthy conclusion to a great album from one of music's great veterans. The song has some of the grandeur that characterized Cohen's music on \\"I'm Your Man\\" and \\"The Future\\".<br /><br />\\"Amen\\" has by many been brought forward as the key track. A must-read for Stones fans, LIFE deserves a special place on the shelf holding the most definitive documentations of rock 'n' roll as a lifestyle.

0812992792|5.0|Jun Do  is the Orphan Master's Son, growing up without an identity with a martyr's name.  He is trained as a tunnel soldier, experiences life as a kidnapper and hero.  The central figure in this astounding book represents a everyman citizen of the shadowy totalitarian state of North Korea.  I was prepared by the reactions of others that this would be a page turner, but had no idea just how compelling a read it would turn out to be.  Identities are discarded and assumed with no attempt at connection to the truth.  The structure when deciphered makes perfect sense, even if odd juxtapositioning of times and events seems arbitrary.  As Jun Do's story unfolds, the timeline shifts, narrators take turns.  Several chapters are narrated in agitprop by omnipresent loud speakers.  `In this book Adam Johnson demonstrates how it is possible for entire populations accept live in unspeakable conditions seemingly willingly and without question. Wielding incessant control the government hammers home the benign character of the North Korean society, claiming that capitalism forces its citizens to live lives of horror and deprivation.  There are sections that are difficult to process, but they are necessary to the story's advancement and to put in for shock value.  Highly recommended.

B0010SKUYM|5.0|As far as I'm concerned, there are three mystical places in the world:  the desert outside Santa Fe; the tree of life in the Arab emirates of Bahrain; and the restaurant at Sunset and Crescent, because that's where I first met her and touched her. ~ Steve Martin, L.A. Story  It's a funny quote for a book review such as this, but for me it's oddly appropriate.  I was living in Los Angeles at the time that I discovered The Name of the Wind.  I picked it up off of a table at the Borders on Sunset and Vine.  The thing is, I had never read a fantasy novel before.  I was convinced that I didn't like big long fantasy novels (though I do love big long other types of books).  Yet for reasons still not entirely clear to myself, I bought it without reading a single page (another rarity).  Then it sat on my bookshelf for over a year until I moved back to Chicago.  Then one cold and windy night I picked it up for no particular reason, intent on reading a few pages so that at the very least I could declare my purchase a waste and justify putting it in the donation box.  Obviously that never happened.  I TORE through this book.  (Okay, actually by then I had a Kindle so after getting hooked in about three chapters I bought the eBook and tore through that).  I devoured it, loving it more than anything I had read in at least a year.  I dreaded the ending because I knew that in addition to being the first in a series, this was a book that would leave a hole in me, the kind of emptiness that comes when you know that no matter what you read next, you're not going to enjoy it as much as what you're reading now-- and that even if you reread what you're currently reading, you'll never fully experience the joy of discover that comes with the first time through.  Hyperbole aside, it really is that good.  Pat Rothfuss is my new hero, but he's also a bastard because his writing is elegant and effortless and so damn good that casts a shadow over my own writing because I know I'll never be thisgood.  I strongly recommend buying at least one additional copy, preferably a few, to distribute to friends after you start reading.  That way you won't have to wait as long for them to finish so you have someone to talk to about it.  But enough gushing from me.  Order this now and enjoy your first read of one of your new favorite books, you lucky bastard.  I'm jealous just thinking about it.

0345457692|3.0|Summary:  In the the 26th century, the human race has expanded beyond planet Earth.  We are now populating planets all over the universe, and the U.N. has become the governing body for law enforcement and conflict resolution.  As part of this program, they developed a group of enforcers, known as envoys, who are programmed for violence and war.  Takeshi Kovaks is a former U.N. envoy.  As such, he was trained to kill ruthlessly and without hesitation.  The most notable scientific advancement in this new world is the development of a program that allows people to download their minds and souls into a device called a cortical stack.  This stack can be stored indefinitely and then implanted in any available human or synthetic body (or sleeve.)  This process allows people to live forever, as long as they continue to download themselves into new sleeves when needed.  However, the process if very expensive, so it is really only the wealthy who can afford this process multiple times.  After being killed on his home planet of Harlan's Way, Kovaks' stack is placed in storage to serve a criminal sentence.  Surprisingly, he finds himself released early and implanted in a male body on Old Earth - a planet he has never visited before.  He has been brought to this planet by a wealthy man who needs his help to solve his own murder.  The task seems simple enough - prove that the man did not commit suicide.  But Kovaks is only on the planet for a few hours before someone is already holding a gun to his head, and he soon discovers that the layers of evil run very deep on this planet.  His nature won't allow him to quit his assignment, but he could lose his last life in the process.  Review:  I really liked the structure of this new futuristic world.  The concept of the stacks was intriguing.  I especially liked how the author described what happens to people when they have lived hundreds of years.  Their humanity, in many ways, is lost over time.  They may stick to their customs and activities, but they can no longer feel a connection or identify with people who are living their first lives.  The result is an enormous separation between the upper and lower classes, not only because of money, but because of centuries of time and experience.  I wasn't quite as interested in the mystery as I wish I had been.  As much as I enjoyed Kovaks, I didn't really care for any of the other characters in the book.  They were all morally depraved to some extent, with some being far worse than others.  I kept wishing that Kovaks would just give up this investigation.  Who cares if this filthy old rich man died of suicide or murder?  He had a new body waiting for him and came right back to life.  A good portion of the other characters were either trying to kill Kovaks or manipulate him, so there was no need to stick around for them either.  Although I hate to admit this, I had a challenge keeping all of the characters straight after a while.  With people changing sleeves and coming back to life, I confused a couple of the characters with each other.  Every once in a while, I'd have to backtrack to figure out where a character was originally introduced, and this was definitely a little frustrating.  I am newly experimenting with Science Fiction, so I am by no means an expert in this genre, nor do I have a lot to compare this book to.  For that reason, I'm focusing my review only on the basic elements of the book:  Great world building with many creative features.  The mystery plotline was extremely complicated, but not all that interesting to me due to the unlikable characters involved.  Overall, this was just an OK read for me.
