B005LFA1DK|4.0|The year is 2030, and you are listening to you father tell you the story of how he met your mother. Over a span of seven seasons with an average of 22 episodes a season, your father retells stories of sitting around the bar with his best friends, describes his various relationships with what seems like hundreds of girls who rarely stick around for long. As soon as you think he's finally at the end of his story, it turns out it's just \\"some girl\\" and not the conclusion of the TV series. But viewers are content with that. The longer it takes Josh Radnor to find his character's wife in How I Met Your Mother, the more television fans get to watch.  What began as a show loosely based off of the producers' experiences in New York City (Carter Bays and Craig Thomas originally wanted to write a sitcom about \\"our friends and the stupid stuff we did in New York\\") quickly became a successful show receiving high ratings and winning five Emmy awards and a nomination for \\"Outstanding Comedy Series\\" in 2009. Along with quite a few other awards and nominations, CBS' How I Met Your Mother was rated the top fifth TV series of the last decade (2002-2012).  The show stars Radnor, as Ted Mosby, and his four best friends Marshall Eriksen (Jason Segel), Marshall's wife, Lily Aldrin (Alyson Hannigan), Robin Scherbatsky (Cobie Smulders), and Barney Stinson (Neil Patrick Harris). Other friends come and go through the seasons but none stay as long as those listed above. Even those characters disappear from time to time, but (SPOILER ALERT!) they always find a way to make it back to MacLaren's, the bar where much of the show is set. As the relationships between the main cast members develop, fans can see the strength of the actors who play them.  The diversity of the characters portrayed in the show allows people with varying views on relationships have an interest. Ted, on one hand, has been looking for his soul mate ever since he realized his two best friends found theirs (with each other),Marshall and Lily found true love while dating for ten years, and Robin, while not against dating, is fully convinced she will never get married or have children. Barney is the man-whore of the group. By making up far-fetched stories about his life, he manages to sleep with a new woman each night. Together, the group collaborates to create a nice blend of different views.  Each character has a strong hold on the group dynamic; without him or her, the group could easily fall apart. This is mostly thanks to the strength of the actors themselves (and of course to the writers, producers, directors etc.). Each actor is able to portray their character in the most humourous way. While still carrying some essence of serenity and seriousness, Radnor, Segel, Hannigan, Smulders, and Harris work together to make an award winning show, loved throughout the country.  While all of the main cast is best known for this hilarious show, Radnor is also well-known for his work as producer, director, and writer of various other productions. His movie HappyThankYouMorePlease (which he wrote, directed, and starred in) won \\"Audience Award\\" before being nominated for \\"Grand Jury Prize\\" at the 26th Sundance Film Festival in 2010. Within the first five minutes of HappyThankYouMorePlease, Radnor, who sports a scruffy beard, untucked button-up shirt and acts as Sam Wexler, shows similar characteristics to his character on How I Met Your Mother: Stopping mid-stride to gawk at a pretty girl, most likely thinking she's \\"the one.\\" Unfortunately, she probably isn't. Even just having a simple \\"Hello, I'm Sam\\" conversation is awkward, as are many of the encounters between Ted Mosby and other women on the show. Whether a secret or not, both Wexler and Mosby are looking for true love and the beauty that tags along for the ride.  The movie made a mere $0.2 million in the box office and the reviews seem to reflect the total revenue. The plot is choppy and unorganized, jumping between what seem like three somehow related characters, much like Valentine's Day and Love Actually. However, instead of bringing everyone together in the end to connect all the strings, the stories were left severed and the audience confused. Radnor's writing was poor and his ideas weren't portrayed well through the characters, unlike How I Met Your Mother where his acting, while often juvenile and sarcastic, is effective in creating a character worth listening to.  After about half of HappyThankYouMorePlease, I found myself wondering how I was going to get through the remainder of the movie. In comparison to my beloved TV show, this movie was below my standards. I found myself hoping that I wouldn't find that Radnor is type-casted, something that seems to happen all too often in cultural media. I wanted Radnor to show his versatility through HappyThankYouMorePlease and How I Met Your Mother. No such luck. I was stuck with the horny man searching the streets of New York for love. Radnor may complete the five-way friendship in How I Met Your Mother with his hunky cuteness, but his performance in HappyThankYouMorePlease is inadequate and doesn't show the strength I had hoped to find. Even in the soon-to-be-released Liberal Arts (also written and directed by and starring Radnor) is clearly of the same origin. Lonely, scruffy, 30-something year old  man lives life by finding love. In this case, it's in a 19 year old college student.  How I Met Your Mother is an entirely entertaining show about drunken escapades around New York City, and if you're looking for that, great. If you're looking for Radnor's breakthrough, hoping to find him taking on a new and refreshing role, you won't find it. However, if you're satisfied with the \\"mega good-looking,\\" sweet, sentimental dude that every girl hopes to marry, you're set for a night in with some chocolate ice cream and a cozy fire.

She acts as the Media Liaison for the team and selects the cases for the team to work. As Hotchner once said, \\"No one on the team is replaceable.\\"  The BAU is so successful because of the perfect balance of the team members.  Jeff Davis created the characters to ensure the variety of personality types and life experience.  Each episode begins and concludes with a quote by an influential figure.  The quotes establish a little context for what will be happening and also summarize the episode. From a viewer's standpoint, the quotes not only apply to the episodes but are usually applicable to daily life. This connection between viewer and television help establish a personal viewing experience. By doing this, the audience feels more emotionally connected to the victims and the members of the BAU.  Serial rapists and pedophiles pull on the heart strings of the audience by pinpointing several emotions: empathy, anticipation, and pure fear. Jeff Davis, creator and producer of season one, is in the early stage of his career. Besides creating Criminal Minds, Davis has created, directed, and produced the new MTV show, Teen Wolf, a teen romance about werewolves. 0765365278|5.0|The Way of Kings is the first book in a projected ten-part epic fantasy series (The Stormlight Archive) by Brandon Sanderson. After reading other Sanderson books (the Mistborn series, Wheel of Time), I was almost certain that I would fall in love with this book, and so I didn't really want to start it, because I liked being able to look forward to reading it. Unfortunately, that plan didn't work, and now I have to wait a year or more for the next book to be released.  So yeah, I thought The Way of Kings was a fantastic book. I'm always at a loss to describe what I like about good books because I get so immersed in the world and characters that it's hard to pick out things as being outstanding. The world was richly drawn, with detailed mythologies, history, countries, peoples, etc. The main characters were all fascinating, three-dimensional people with compelling stories. I liked the description of the Vorin society - it's interesting that women have their own specialties and talents, as opposed to a lot of traditional fantasy. After reading Mistborn, with its clear descriptions of the (really cool) magic systems, I expected something similar, but magic is very much shrouded in mystery, and that's pretty well portrayed. I love the amount of scholarship depicted in this book.  I found that the middle dragged a tiny bit as Kaladin sorted out his emotions and accepted being a leader, but I think that's a curse of first books in series' - the hero has to go on a believable journey. It also seemed like a whole bunch of plot twists came at the very end; I would have appreciated it if there were a couple of those to liven up the middle of the book. However, it did succeed in making me really impatient for the next installment of the story.

0525478817|4.0|~4.5/5<br />So, I'm feeling a little unsure about this book. And so I'm not totally sure what to say about it. I feel like I kind of don't think it was amazing, but it was amazing. And there is no reason for me to not think it was amazing. Maybe it was just so much, and I'm overwhelmed and very unsure or something. Also, I feel a little biased and pressured, and like I should only say amazing things about it. But I also don't feel like there's really anything not amazing about it in the first place. And I don't really know what rating to give it. I feel like, maybe, it doesn't really deserve a 4.5, and yet it also deserves nothing less?<br />I'm just all kinds of conflicted. (But, I swear, I'm going to be honest throughout all of this review.) (Also, I promise, I'm not going to spoil anything big in this besides general plot. So, Spoiler Free!)<br />So I understand what John Green (I kind of want to address him as John and not Green. But that's unprofessional, isn't it? [Not that, you know, I am professional or anything.] And yet, as a Nerdfighter, that's what I always think of him as. Maybe I'll just call him John Green through all of it?) was trying to say with all of it. I get the message. And it was awesome and completely well done. And I might talk more about it later, but I don't think that's what I'm going to focus on in this review.<br />I am going to talk about Hazel, though. Ahh, Hazel Grace. First of all, I want to say how well John did at creating a real girl. Especially because, as a (you know, mostly) normal teenage(-ish) girl, I related to her in a lot of ways. She's a bit cynical, and not all that sure of herself, and she says 'um' quite a bit and ends a lot of what she says in a question. And I love how she thinks about boys, and just living in general. And she's smart, and in college even though she's sixteen (with a completely understandable and logical reason), and she's honest (at least to herself). She's a teenager, even if she does have cancer.<br />And, ohhh, Augustus Waters. (Also, I want to mention that I love the names John Green came up with. They are brilliant.) I don't even... But I do, I do know what to say about him. He is simply amazing. He wants to mean something, and he's fun, and he likes Hazel and doesn't see why she shouldn't know this. He's just... full of swoon. He is all swoon. There is so much swoon with him that I don't even know what to tell you besides that you have to read it to fully understand the amazing-ness that is him and Hazel, when they are together and not. And I like that the drama wasn't pumped up very much or anything. Even when they have problems (that would usually cause a big scene or something), it isn't too tense, it's not dramatic. It just is what it is.<br />And I wasn't expecting what... happened to him, to happen to him. Especially not before her. The situation was different from what I'd expected when I started the book, and I'm glad. It turned out different, in a  very good way. Well, not very, but, you know...<br />Also, you guys. I'd read several reviews and seen lots of people talking about it, and how it was full of all these emotions. And, since I'm not the type of person that cries at things, I was like, 'yea, okay', but my interest was piqued. And you guys, everyone was kind of right. A lot of the book is just fun and more fun, and there are a ton of cute moments between Hazel Grace and Augustus. It's much more funny than I thought it would be, like with moments of the three just joking around, which I really enjoyed. And, yea, there are several moments in the book where Hazel is by herself and thinking about things, and I enjoyed them, but I didn't think they were overwhelmingly upsetting or anything. But then. But then, you guys. A scene came up about Augustus. And I was completely caught off guard.<br />I knew it was coming; it was obvious that it was coming. And even when it started, I was like 'aww, sad', but then, and I think I might have just put myself in Hazel's mind and imagined it for myself, but then I thought 'oh, there are tears in my eyes'. I'm not even kidding. This book brought me so close to crying, closer than anything I've read before. And it was amazing. Sad, but amazing.<br />And then there's Isaac. He's one of their friends, who goes blind. But him and Augustus play violent video games together, and then him and Hazel play vocal video games, and he has a moment of denial and hard realization that's a bit sad (and honest, and I genuinely enjoyed it), and he's just generally awesome.<br />We didn't see too much of anyone else's parents (although what we did see was usually pretty humorous or sad) besides Hazel's, who we saw often. She's close to both of them, and they were pretty awesome. Her dad, in particular, was so sweet, and very sensitive (and cried often). And she jokes around easily with her mom, for instance, when they take a trip with Augustus. Near the end, they got a bit pushy, but otherwise were pretty cool.<br />Also, the subject matter. It made me a little wary, but it didn't put me off from reading it or anything, because I was much too excited for that. And I haven't even read much about people with cancer, or many novels with sick people. But he did it really well. It was (I'm assuming this, because I haven't, nor have I ever been around someone who has, experienced this,) realistic. And I'm pretty sure he knew what he was talking about, as there were several technical words and stuff thrown around, and I know that he did a lot of research (not that that he couldn't have been making it all up, though). He wasn't afraid of showing the ugly parts of it. It seemed very honest, with the way everyone reacted to it, and thought about living and dying, and how they're all treated because they're sick, like with the Perks and Support Group. (And I liked the, \\"depression is a side effect of dying\\" thing, in particular. There were several parts of the book that made me think, \\"That's a nice/interesting way of looking at things\\". There are just so many little things that matter so much in this book.)<br />And whole thing with Van Houten and An Imperial Affliction. That's just... hm. (Kind of messy? And a bit sad?) There's the big similarities. With the endings, for example. And it is a bit frustrating, but I get it. So, fine. Fine.<br />The book has John Green's general way of writing. It's his style. He's a bit subtle and honest and a little dark; just him. This book included text messages and letters and little things like that. 1401937519|5.0|Up and coming author Anita Moorjani's debut book Dying To Be Me is undoubtedly one of the most important books of 2012. It is one of the most inspiring memoirs I have ever read. In these pages Anita shares in the most touching and heart felt of ways her personal history, growing up different, the tension cultural expectations created within her and the events leading up to her getting cancer and experiencing the most magnificent of near death experiences at the time her organs began to shut down in the final hours of advanced stage Hodgkin's Lymphoma.<br /> During her NDE Anita awakened to a heightened state of awareness which allowed her to understand why she had cancer (among many other things) and accessed a state of unconditional loving energy that allowed her to gain full recovery of her health.<br /><br />I need not elaborate on Anita's story any further, as it can be read in her NDE account at the Near Death Experience Research Foundation website, and there are also many different interviews available  online for those who are unfamiliar with her story. However, if you are looking for a clich&eacute; NDE book with tunnels, white lights, angels and God you will be disappointed. This NDE transcends all cultures and all religions.<br /><br />I would like to emphasize that despite being a book about a near death experience, it has a much broader appeal than most other NDE books and is in my opinion multi-disciplinary. It is of huge value to anyone interested in the areas of near death experiences, death, dying and bereavement,  cancer, spontaneous remission, the mind-body relationship, mind-body and energy medicine, reincarnation, or to anyone who simply wishes to examine how the universe and life works in general. It even makes a compelling case for the skeptics of spiritual phenomena, as her spontaneous remission so close to death is one of a kind and medically documented and verified.  In addition to Anita's wonderful message, I would hope that her views on cancer provide food for thought for oncologists around the world, and change the way the world and in particular the medical profession thinks about, views and treats cancer.<br /><br />What impresses me most about this book and about Anita's message is that her NDE pertains much more to life and living than it does to death and what we can expect in the afterlife (although this topic is also covered in the book). It helps us eliminate the clutter in our lives and to focus more on what makes us happy and brings us joy in life, as well as eliminating any existential phobias we may have stemming from the relatively unknown realm of death.<br /><br />Often spiritual books can create more existential fears and tensions rather than alleviate them with mention of concepts of sin, karma, pre-life planning of car accidents and illnesses, \\"soul lessons\\", soul levels, hell, punishment etc. These books usually have some sort of agenda to push, but luckily this is not the case with Anita Moorjani, she is a breath of fresh air, something I am eternally grateful for.<br /><br />Anita doesn't claim to be a spiritual guru, she doesn't peddle any sort of agenda as many other spiritual authors do. She has no dogma to force onto the reader. Instead she has an amazing story to tell (which she does with amazing dexterity in the English language), and shares from her NDE the many insights she gained into her own life, her own cancer and life and living in general, insights that anyone can benefit from.<br /><br />For me Anita's NDE and the insight she gained from it tremendously helped me overcome much cognitive dissonance which came from reading various New Age spiritual, self help type books, and put life into a much  clearer and more logical perspective. At the same time Anita offers an outlook on life that is relatively easy to implement-DO WHAT FEELS RIGHT FOR YOU, DON'T DO WHAT DOESN'T FEEL RIGHT FOR YOU, everything else in life is secondary.<br /><br /> If you are embarking on a quest for spiritual knowledge or if you have been on one for many years, don't buy 30 New Age books on soul mates, twin flames, soul lessons, law of attraction, indigo children, soul progression, karma etc, such books have little to do with the greater cosmic reality and in my opinion, for the most part, the concepts discussed in them are misconceptions at best, in other cases just plain lies. Such books can create quite a destructive and toxic belief system and can make living life more difficult and complicated than it needs to be.<br /><br />Instead, just buy this book. It will help you remove the clutter from your mind, your way of thinking and from your life as well. The biggest myth that Dying To Be Me dismisses is that being spiritual or living life needs to be hard work, that we need to work hard for a favoured position in the afterlife, that we need to suffer and make sacrifices for spiritual advancement, enlightenment and perfection (such beliefs are the foundation of all religions and New Age thought).  In the absence of such ideas Anita provides us with a much lighter and fulfilling perspective on life, allowing us to become more effective at being ourselves.<br /><br />In Anita's own words \\"If you think you need to work hard at being spiritual then you haven't got it yet.\\"<br /><br />Dying To Be Me is undoubtedly a ten star book, a book with universal appeal and with a message that will never lose its relevance to spirituality and to living a happy and fulfilled life.st for spiritual knowledge or if you have been on one for many years, don't buy 30 New Age books on soul mates, twin flames, soul lessons, law of attraction, indigo children, soul progression, karma etc, such books have little to do with the greater cosmic reality and in my opinion, for the most part, the concepts discussed in them are misconceptions at best, in other cases just plain lies. B000001DTM|4.0|Suddenly becoming one of the most universally recognized bands in the world after the release of \\"The Joshua Tree\\", U2 began to use their newly-found widespread fame to focus public attention on a variety of issues centering around universal appeals to fairness, justice, and equality that often descended into politically charged jeremiads which were often polarizing in nature.  The band also began to incorporate distinctive elements associated with traditional American musical genres into the arrangements of their own songs with results that were, at times, decidedly mixed.  After the release of \\"Rattle And Hum\\" a double-length cassette/CD comprised of material especially composed for that recording and live versions of tracks drawn primarily from \\"The Joshua Tree\\", their polemical remarks, coupled with a creeping projection of deadly earnestness, were beginning to erode the newest additions to their fanbase (and trying the patience of longtime supporters), and threatened to jeopardize their position as a band whose growth seemed both organic and an evolving phenomenon. As the band began the recording process for their studio follow-up to \\"The Joshua Tree\\", their challenge in a very real sense was to make a positive adjustment to the manner in which the musical landscape was changing around them, and adapt to it.  \\"Achtung Baby\\", the result of that process, was U2's blatant if creative attempt at remaining relevant and contemporary, a release that, while still qualitatively identifiable as material that had been recorded by U2, radically refined many of the signature elements that had previously been associated with their distinctive sound. And perhaps the feature most radicaly transformed was Edge's guitar work,  previously distinguished by its subtle, atmospheric, textural , heavily-delayed ringing harmonic, and here a sonically assaultive force, heavily phased,flanged, fuzzed-out, and distorted according to the need of each track, or perhaps even within a singletrack. Now the band and its production team also opted to bring Adam Clayton's bassline, and Larry Mullen, Jr.'s percussive work to the front of the mix and syncopate them much more heavily to push many of the tracks forward with a propulsive groove, perhaps drawing inspiration from the resurgence of club music (think the Manchester Scene) and streamlined, newly accessible, third-generation industrial music (cue up Nine Inch Nails). Honestly, there's very little in \\"The Fly\\", the first track the band released as \\"AB\\"'s first single that allowed me to identify it as a U2 song other than Bono's filtered vocals, an elongated guitar line during the breakdown, and the heavy feedback immediately following it punctuated by a burst of arpeggiation.  That's not to say that longtime fans were in completely unfamiliar waters here; \\"One\\" , a mutated ballad and universal constant on their set list over the past 20 years, contains a guitar line in its extended coda that is a partial reversion to Edge's chiming atmospherics, while \\"Trying To Throw Your Arms Around The World\\" even with its offbeat percussive flourishes, could possibly have been culled from \\"The Joshua Tree\\", Edge's guitar lines echoing expansively.  Nevertheless, \\"Achtung Baby\\" is muscular, heavier, and far more densely constructed then any of its predecessors, a sonic collage driven by a massive guitar presence, electronic manipulation, actual, established grooves rather then simple rhythmic insistence, and lyrical content whose subject appears to be love in all of its universal aspects, personal and apolitical. Perhaps not quite as cohesive as \\"The Joshua Tree\\", \\"Achtung Baby\\" is a collection of singles with a looser thematic structure that almost comes across as unified; \\"Until The End Of The World\\", \\"Even Better Than The Real Thing\\", \\"Mysterious Ways\\" all charted here in the United States. \\"Love Is Blindness\\", the closing track, was not released as a single here, but its heavily pulsating bassline, thrumming organ chord, block percussion, and infinite, reverbed guitar line combined with truly poetic lyrics from Bono to make this the most convicting song on the entire recording, and I still remember it as their penultimate number when they performed it as an encore at RFK Stadium during '92's \\"Zoo Station\\" Tour.  I predict that I'll probably take some heat for this, but I consider 'Achtung Baby\\" to be the last internally consistent U2 release, and wish sometimes that the band had made it their last. I rank it just behind \\"The Joshua Tree\\" as their masterwork, though I'll always retain a softer spot for their earlier material. I can strongly recommend this CD, but get their first 5, in order, to get a sense of how their sound evolved.

0786955570|4.0|---Introduction---<br />After hearing it plugged on the Wizards of the Coast D&D podcast, I made the Dungeons & Dragons: Castle Ravenloft Board Game a last-minute addition to my Christmas Wishlist this past year despite concerns that the \\"Dungeons & Dragons\\" would scare most of my friends away from giving it a try. To my pleasant surprise, I received it and have already played a number of enjoyable games. Not only has Castle Ravenloft been well-received by my D&D gamer-friends, but even by those who had never seen a d20 before - a huge testament to the accessibility of the game and its ability to rise above and beyond the stigma sometimes associated with Dungeons & Dragons. Originally retailing for $65, the price has since dropped to $45 on Amazon.com. (Check out the fully-formatted review at my website)<br /><br />\\"An exciting D&D boardgame for 1-5 players. The master of Ravenloft is having guests for dinner--and you are invited! Evil lurks in the towers and dungeons of Castle Ravenloft, and only heroes of exceptional bravery can survive the horrors within. Designed for 1-5 players, this boardgame features multiple scenarios, challenging quests, and cooperative game play.\\"<br /><br />Included Components - 8.5<br /><br />* 40 plastic heroes and monsters: color-coded for quick identification(no digging through pieces)<br />* 13 sheets of interlocking cardstock dungeon tiles: thick cardstock will allow for repeated handling<br />* 200 encounter and treasure cards: thick cardstock keeps the cards from easily bending and prevent them from blowing away (you've all had a sneeze like that, admit it)<br />* Rulebook<br />* Scenario book<br />* 20-sided die<br /><br />Though the materials supplied are adequate in terms of quality as well as quantity, there is no explanation as to what some of the elements are used for. Yes, the miniatures are color-coded, but for the rest of the pieces, only by playing the game, do you discover that there are special pieces unique to individual adventures. Not a big deal, but something that could have been addressed to save us some confusion.<br /><br />---Mechanics/Ease of Play - 9.0---<br /><br />\\"It's fun and really easy - there's just 3 things to do on my turn\\" - My wife.<br /><br />That quote from my wife, though an oversimplification, is really a great indicator for the simple, but enjoyable mechanics that drive the gameplay of Castle Ravenloft and drive-home the point that you don't have to know anything about Dungeons & Dragons to have a great time(though you'll certainly be a step ahead if you're already familiar with the d20 system).<br /><br />Setup<br /><br />The included scenario book provides about 20 varied adventures for the players (from 1 to 5 of them) to tackle cooperatively. Each scenario has a set of victory and failure conditions that tie each of the Heroes(as the players are called) together in pursuit of a specific goal. Each scenario has specific directions to facilitate crafting the game board appropriately. In general, the dungeon(game board) is pieced together at the players direction as tiles, monsters, encounters and treasures are drawn randomly from shuffled piles. Not only does this provide for some great replay value, but it also adds an element of suspense as players have no idea what is lurking around the \\"next corner\\".<br /><br />Each player chooses one of the 5 provided heroes to play during the course of the scenario: Cleric, Fighter, Ranger, Rogue or Wizard. Each of the heroes has a corresponding \\"hero tile\\" that contains all of the details specific to that \\"class\\". Of particular note are the values for Armor Class(AC), Speed, Hit Points(HP) and Surge Value(a concept Wizards of the Coast first introduced with D&D 4e). Each player then chooses a set of 4 to 6 \\"powers\\" from a deck of about 10 hero-specific cards. Each deck consists of various at-will, daily and utility powers. At-will powers can be used repeatedly throughout the scenario, whereas daily and utility powers are expended upon use and only brought back into play by certain treasure items. Helpfully, Wizards included a \\"recommended\\" list of power card for each hero that I have found to be very effective, while still providing advanced players with the opportunity to \\"fine tune\\" their powers based on specific scenarios.<br /><br />Your turn<br /><br />Players collectively decide who will go first and play then goes around the table. Each turn consists of three \\"phases\\" that dictate the flow of each turn:<br /><br />The Hero Phase<br /><br />The first part of your turn, the hero phase is just what it sounds like - where the Hero takes the spotlight. During this phase you will be moving your hero miniature around the game board and attacking monsters that you discover. You can use one \\"attack\\" power card per turn, as well as utility powers as indicated by the description. Your movements are nearly as important as your attacks, allowing you to \\"explore\\" new areas of the dungeon in the exploration phase to follow, avoid traps, or flee from monsters. The relatively small number of powers to choose from combined with simple rules for movement makes your choices feel meaningful without getting bogged down in a tedium of rules deliberation. I found the powers very easy to use, while maintaining the elements of choice so important to the RPG genre.<br /><br />Based on the d20 system, each attack involves rolling a twenty-sided die, adding a modifier and comparing the result to the Armor Class(AC) of the target. If the roll plus modified is equal or greater than the AC, your attack hits the target and deals damage; otherwise, your attack misses and usually has no effect.<br /><br />The Exploration Phase<br /><br />The dungeon is built primarily in the exploration phase based on your actions during the Hero phase based on the concept of an \\"unexplored edge\\". Simply put, any dungeon tile edge that is not depicted as a wall, nor already connected to another dungeon tile is considered to be an \\"unexplored edge\\" and thus, ripe for hero exploration. If the hero phase ends with your miniature positions adjacent to an unexplored edge, you draw a new dungeon tile and place it next to your hero. In addition, you draw a card from the \\"Monster\\" deck and place the appropriate miniature on the new tile on top of the pile of skulls that each dungeon tiles has. Each of the Monster cards has all of the stats(AC, HP) and tactics(how it attacks and moves) listed similar to the Hero tiles that you choose from before the game starts. This is important - in Castle Ravenloft YOU will control each of the monsters that you draw from the deck until they are killed by your party of heroes(or less-pleasantly, until you are killed by them).<br /><br />The Villain Phase<br /><br />If you did not draw a new dungeon tile during the exploration phase or if you drew a dungeon tile marked with a black triangle (anecdotally about 50% of the tiles are black and 50% are white, though I haven't actually counted them) you will also draw an \\"Encounter\\" card. These Encounter cards are always negative events, that will impact one or more of the heroes in some way. Most encounters are some type of attack against all heroes in the current heroes dungeon tile; this could be a one-time attack, or it could be a persistent \\"trap\\" device that is activated along with monsters you may also control. In addition to straight-up attacks, you can also find \\"Environment\\" effects that last until another \\"Environment\\" card is drawn - these can do anything from reducing all heroes' speed by 1 to forcing you to roll twice for every attack and use the lower result. These cards can be quite devastating when the heroes are clumped together on the same tile, so they force you to spread out or risk a catastrophic event. During gameplay, the Encounters really increased the pacing and level of urgency.<br /><br />After you determine that an Encounter card should/should not be drawn, you activate all of the monsters and traps under your control in the order they were drawn. Far less arbitrary than it sounds, a player reads the concise \\"tactics\\" for each Monster or Trap<br /><br />The d20<br /><br />Ah, the d20 (20-sided die) - bringer of fortune and failure alike - it is the foundational element in Castle Ravenloft and what really ties the mechanics to Dungeons & Dragons.<br /><br />---Fun Factor - 8.0---<br /><br />Despite a somewhat slow start(due to setup, reading/explaining the \\"adventure\\", etc.), the game quickly picks up the pace into what can only be described as a frantic, entertaining fight to survive that only ends when you've completed the heroic objective or have died trying! Your success (or failure) is based almost entirely on how well (or poorly) you managed the constant barrage of monsters, traps, and events that are thrown at the party. Fail to control the onslaught and you will be quickly overrun as the monsters reach an unstoppable critical mass.<br /><br />The designers have done an excellent job of making every adventure take you to cusp of destruction when you finally reach your objective and (hopefully) emerge from the Castle Ravenloft dungeons as victorious heroes.<br /><br />However, after a few games I did find that my selection of actions(during the Hero phase) seemed to grow less interesting. A larger selection of power cards would be a welcomed addition and would likely remedy this minor drawback.<br /><br />---Craftsmanship/Art - 7.0---<br /><br />The craftsmanship is at a higher level than I expected(due to low expectations), but seems appropriate considering the cost of the game. The game tiles and hero cards, in particular, have a nice weight to them that lends to the impression of high-caliber materials. One notable exception, are the miniatures - outside of the \\"main bad guys\\"(which have decent quality), the miniatures seem cheap and prone to breakage. The hero miniatures in particular, bend easily and one or two of them fall over easily. This is one area for improvement, especially considering the cost of the game.<br /><br />The art direction is consistent with the rest of the Dungeons & Dragons product line. For those not familiar, it is fairly typical fantasy art with an emphasis on \\"realistic portrayal\\", as opposed to the over-exaggerated caricatures found in anime, etc. Suitable for developing the mood of the game, I found it to be quite adequate - nothing more, nothing less.<br /><br />There was a lot of room for Wizards to \\"WOW!\\" me with the craftsmanship and art here - sadly, they didn't take full advantage. It's more of a \\"nitpick\\" for me, but as the only real \\"flaw\\" in the game, I felt it is worth mentioning.<br /><br />---Replay Value - 9.0---<br /><br />The very nature of the game(building the game board as play progresses) results in a new experience every time, even when playing the same \\"adventure\\". Combinations of hero classes and the inclusion of 20 unique adventures provide abundant material for even the most avid gamer. I have owned the game for over a year now and have yet to even play through all of the adventures once, much-less play through them repeatedly.<br /><br />Unlike many board games that fall into predictable patterns of play, the designers of Castle Ravenloft have managed to create a game that feels familiar to those with experience without succumbing to repetition, boredom, and ultimately disinterest.<br /><br />If you and your gaming group are looking for a game to hold your interests through multiple gatherings, Castle Ravenloft is one that I highly recommend!<br /><br />---Conclusion - 8.3 Great! Highly Recommended Purchase.--<br /><br />The mechanics and replay value really set Dungeons & Dragons: Castle Ravenloft apart from other games and serve to polish and balance the \\"meh\\" art and craftsmanship that would have otherwise detracted from the overall appeal. A job well done and an excellent addition to your gaming repertoire that will last through many gatherings. As an added benefit, Ravenloft also serves as a great tool for introducing otherwise hesitant friends to the Dungeons & Dragons RPG - which otherwise has a fairly high learning curve.and failure alike - it is the foundational element in Castle Ravenloft and what really ties the mechanics to Dungeons & Dragons.  ---Fun Factor - 8.0---  Despite a somewhat slow start(due to setup, reading/explaining the \\"adventure\\", etc.), the game quickly picks up the pace into what can only be described as a frantic, entertaining fight to survive that only ends when you've completed the heroic objective or have died trying! If you and your gaming group are looking for a game to hold your interests through multiple gatherings, Castle Ravenloft is one that I highly recommend!  ---Conclusion - 8.3 Great! Highly Recommended Purchase.--  The mechanics and replay value really set Dungeons & Dragons: Castle Ravenloft apart from other games and serve to polish and balance the \\"meh\\" art and craftsmanship that would have otherwise detracted from the overall appeal. A job well done and an excellent addition to your gaming repertoire that will last through many gatherings. As an added benefit, Ravenloft also serves as a great tool for introducing otherwise hesitant friends to the Dungeons & Dragons RPG - which otherwise has a fairly high learning curve.

B0012GMV58|5.0|Carlos Santana has maintained a very adaptable construct within the Santana band,allowing a signature sound to continue but a variety of different musicians to come in and out. So it was all more a musical collective than a band in terms of personnel maintenance. For his part Carlos himself always had a musical kinship with John Coltrane and was during the early 70's looking to develop a guitar personality similar to what Coltrane was seeking to do with his saxophone. And that was to allow the music to come from within his heart and soul as opposed to trying to force the music out. This way he could communicate spiritually to the people of the world without playing over their heads. Santana admired this. And in 1973 he paired up with Mahavishnu Orchestra founder John McLaughlin,formerly of Miles Davis's (and of course Miles had once worked with Coltrane) for the album [[ASIN:B0012GMW34 Love Devotion Surrender]]. After this he put together a new Santana band with McLaughlin along with vocalists Leon Thomas,Flora Purim and keyboardist/percussionist Tom Coster. This band had an even more expansive and jazzier approach than the Woodstock era Santana band had. And it would seem Santana was in the state to pursue the more soul satisfying elements of his music than the commerical ones.<br /><br />  It's important to understand that just because this album isn't overtly commercial in nature doesn't mean it won't catch the listen. Vocally and instrumentally,this album is bursting from front to back with endless layers of rhythm and melody. The musicianship on the songs are...well it goes without saying. And actually it bursts out with the same free-for-all percussive free jazz-funk fusion bag right from the start with \\"Going Home,an firey organ led  musical sermon co-written with Alice Coltrane. Leon Thomas lends his vocals to the slickly grooving \\"Love,Devotion And Surrender\\" as well as \\"When I Look Into Your Eyes\\",which is a swelling soul/funk number with a very dramatic melody and percussion tune.. On \\"Yours Is The Light\\" Flora Purim brings her Brazilian flavored vocal turns to the affair. Leon Thomas is back again on the almost cinematic orchestral groove of \\"Light Of Life\\",again with beautiful swelling melodic chords and a thick rhythm section. The kalimba powered \\"Mother Afria\\",\\"Flame Sky\\" and the title song all all showcase similar types of latin jazz-funk style percussive instrumentals of a more flamboyant variation on the sound the bands [[ASIN:B0000A2I1B Caravanserai]] album achieved. There's also the bonus track \\"Mantra\\",a largely instrumental rhythmic meditation where only words such as joy,love and peace are chanted as vocal elements.<br /><br />  While I must say the recent Woody Allen film [[ASIN:B005MYEQ4U Midnight in Paris]] correctly (and poetically) points out the psychological dangers of living in ones own pastime paradise,there is a part of me that yearns for an environment where meaningful music with meaningful lyrics such as this isn't perceived as \\"old fashioned\\" and even corny by some people. Now doubt that anyone would refer to Santana in his prime as being corny. But I personally always have this feeling I'll wind up playing this album for someone who'll say \\"well that's great but I've got something by a NEWER group that's even better\\",only to listen to it and feel only more drawn toward this. I know,sounds like a value judgement. It's only pointing out how different some of us are in terms of dealing with creativity. Listening to this I have to admit,if Santana's mission of this album was to serve up food for the soul and it's creative energies,mission accomplished. It takes jazz,funk and latin rock to the ends of their creative limits. And does so with the most elegant and enlightening of grooves,melodic chords and harmonies. Highly recommended not only (obviously) to Santana admirers but to anyone whose not devoutly religious who wants to experience something...as close to thoroughly spiritual as popular music can probably get.t.

B005K0HDGE|4.0|Let me begin by saying that I am not a Stephen King fan. I don't do horror--or scary--or really even weird. The title and a recommendation from my sister had me buying this from Amazon at the highest price I have ever paid for an e-book. I guess in King's defense it is also perhaps one of the longest novels I have ever Kindled. (Is that a new verb for you, Webster?)   The title of this book will be a huge clue to the plot for anyone who a) has studied American History; or b) was born prior to November 22, 1963. No one can think of the assassination of President John Kennedy (little John-John saluting the flag on the coffin in the funeral cortege broke just about every heart in America) as anything other than a world-changing event. King has used it as the central point in this book. What if someone could go back and change history? Take out Lee Harvey Oswald before he could make the fatal shot?   High school teacher Jake Epping takes that challenge when he stumbles across a time portal in the back room of a local diner. Sound crazy? Well, if you are reading a novel with a time portal, who's to say what is a little, well maybe more than a little, off. So accept the time portal and settle in for a trip back to the late 50's and early 60's. It will be a bumpy ride and you won't be meeting the rock-around-the-clock gang. King's Dallas is an anti-Kennedy racial hot bed that is a dangerous place for inner-city dwellers.   I found Epping to be a little cold and maybe just a little all-knowing. I would also like to think that King (being from Maine) might have a little anti-south sentiment flowing in his veins. However, It is a very well-researched novel and as the actual facts interplay with King's fictional plot you begin to buy into it and root for Epping.  This is an excellent read--if only for the fact that it makes you think about the \\"what if\\" questions that have haunted us for decades.  You may read this and other reviews at readsquare.com.

0439023513|3.0|Speaking as someone who rather enjoyed this series as a whole, I'll be honest and say that the final book is the lowest in my favorite rankings. In fact, my preference order follows the trilogy's chronological order. Put simply, this one falls a bit below par.  I don't want to give too much away here, so I'll try to be as vague, plot-wise, as possible. This book takes us to a very different place than the previous two, and we see our main characters shift and evolve in all kinds of extreme ways. Some of it, I liked - the dynamic of it all was interesting and kept me engaged. However, much of it was far too extreme for my liking. The polar shift in at least one character was heartbreaking and, to me, didn't serve much of a purpose as an overarching plot function. The various tragedies are too numerous; I hate to say it, but when one of my very favorite secondary characters was killed, I felt a bit numb to it. I attribute that numbness to two factors: first, it was just one of many other deaths in rapid succession, thus leaving a much smaller impact than it could have; and second, it was just done too quickly.  My biggest beef here, however, was the ending itself. To put it simply, what I wanted to happen did, indeed, happen. But Collins managed to write it in a way that was absolutely deflating. There was so much action and movement and progression along a timeline this story that I'm completely mystified by the quick, sloppy conclusion. It's as if Collins was reaching her publisher's upper page number limit, so she chaotically summarized and skipped ahead to the ending of our character's lives. The epilogue is so brief it's almost pointless. I appreciate a book that ends in a complex way, where not all of your questions are answered - but this one answers some questions automatically and others not at all, and the overall impression is negative. Despite the fact that wonderful things happen, we're made to feel like our favorite characters have lost. And what's thatabout?  Honestly, I really did enjoy the series. I finished it all in four days, so I hope you believe me when I say it was fascinating, captivating, and exciting. But we all know that feeling you get when you finish a long book or a strong series - that elation mixed with disappointment and the loss of a new friend. But this ending cut that lovely sensation short for me, and I can't seem to get over that little, hollow feeling it's given me instead.  To conclude: it's a strong series and an action-packed third installment well worth a read - but don't expect to be dazzled by the ending.

0439023491|4.0|We all know the first book of a series is almost impossible to beat. I won't lie to you - that's true for the Hunger Games, too. But, all things considered, this is an unusually strong example of a second installment. (To be honest, I still like it much better than the final installment.)<br /><br />The beginning is a bit slow as we're thrown into the slightly distanced aftermath of the 74th Hunger Games. Our main characters are living at home. And...well, for a little while, that's it. They are not engaged in their relationships or their lifestyles. Understandably, they're scarred; annoyingly, especially in Katniss's case, they are too lazy and uncaring to try and rebuild.<br /><br />Nevertheless, it's a realistic start. As the plot progresses, things begin to change and we become increasingly suspicious. Katniss and Peeta should be finished and living easy lives, shouldn't they? But, of course, this is Panem and the Hunger Games, and nothing is quite that simple. Our conspiratorial dystopia can't just let that happen. So things change, deals are struck, threats are made.<br /><br />I don't want to give anything away here, so I'll try to be vague. To put it simply, this book took exactly the turn I dreaded. I feared repetition; I was afraid this plot would become a dull, replicated version of the first. But honestly, just after that twist turned, I was proven wrong. It was dynamic and it was different, and the consequences were new. The secondary characters were wonderful and I adored Katniss's new acquaintances. The suspense wasn't hard to keep an investment in (although it simply couldn't match that of the first book), and the details were all fresh and original. What a relief that was.<br /><br />Now, I do think there was a bit too much \\"middleness\\" happening here. In writing, you want every scene to have its own story arc and fit into the broader arc of the overall tale. The same should go for installments in a series; each, I believe, should have its own unique storyline with its own progression and climax and conclusion, all of which fit smoothly into the overarching story. I wish that was better done here. There are climaxes, but they are smaller than I'd like; the conclusion is a cliffhanger, which we accept as readers of a trilogy, but not a very satisfying one. Our story arc stays too high; it moves right into the next plotline without fully coming down from the one we just endured.<br /><br />That's an exhausting way to read, if you ask me. I appreciate the newness of everything - but I wish it wasn't thrown in there just to confuse us and make us desperate for the next installment. That's great and all, and it needs to happen for a series to be successful, but we can't ignore the needs of each book along the way.<br /><br />To conclude: a steady increase of risk and twist for the overall series plot and a great new approach to our characters' experiences, but a bit too reliant on building the stakes for the future and not reliant enough on the now.e storyline with its own progression and climax and conclusion, all of which fit smoothly into the overarching story. B002MQYOFW|5.0|Unique, strong, and well-written. So rarely do we find the winning combination of fascinating plot, sustained suspense, and developed characters that this first installment offers!  To begin with, the first few pages of this novel are outstanding. It's a brisk, impactful opening that's individualized enough to inspire my investment, but localized enough to make sure it doesn't answer too many questions too immediately. This is a textbook example of the start-in-the-middle kind of opening I love to find, and it's an excellent way to begin this series. Fantastic.  The premise of this story is both fascinating and tragic, which is an excellent start. But beyond that, it's meaningfully explored and maintains that fascination throughout the novel. Judging from the perspective of a writer myself, this is on my checklist of \\"Things You Must Find in a Good Book.\\" Loved it.  As someone with a general distaste for sci-fi, I've got to say that the elements of scientific unreality are very well injected into this novel. Without pushing too far or diving too deep in specifics we don't really care about, Collins gives us hints of a cruel, futuristic world with horrifying advances but a strong, intact sense of what it means to be human. It is a relatable world of fantasy. Very well done.  It took me less than twenty-four hours to finish this book, and that's for two reasons: the suspense and the emotional investment. Our windows to the brutality of this world are painful; they are spaced well enough to maintain that heightened sense of urgency and have the fullest impact on us as readers. We follow Katniss far beyond the ends of tolerable human ignorance and into undeniable evils as she tries to keep both her survival and her sanity and mind; we follow Peeta as he goes painstakingly about the task of holding on to his identity and preserving the life he loves. The action is quick enough to keep me hooked, but slow enough to hit hard. Scrupulously heartbreaking.  Characters, in anynovel, should be well-rounded and believable enough to capture our hearts. These characters do. I want Peeta to get what he desires so badly; I want Katniss to hold on to what she needs; I want Prim and their mother's suffering to end. Even the secondary characters, like Katniss's Capitol team, are so important to me and stick in my mind so profoundly that I don't want to see them leave the scene. The conflict they instill - should I love them or hate them? and should Katniss appreciate them or resent them? - is very effective. Excellent range of characters.  Really, the only scruple I have with this novel is the grammar. Commas and other punctuation are not always used properly, and I had to reread sentences somewhat frequently to get them to flow through my mind correctly. The imagery and characterization still come across strongly, but had I not faced that distraction, I think it could have been even more intense. We never like to see the voice of the author come through - just that of the characters. So that would be the one thing I'd like to see change.  To conclude: an addictive, powerful opening to a strong series with a few minor technical weaknesses that are almost negligible against a winning big picture.

B006UG90RM|5.0|I was never the biggest Halen fan growing up, but i did like them a lot. I was one of the more heavy abrasive metal fans. I've played in two bands, one that was signed, so heavy music is my forte'. I've always thought Eddie was definitely the catalyst (other than Hendrix) of wild outside the box crazy guitar playing. I'm a big Joe Satriani fan, but i do realize without Eddie there would have probably not been a Joe. At least the one we know today. I've liked both versions of Halen, the Roth days were the heaviest and had some of the best guitar work Eddie has ever put down, my favorite being the first record. Around 86 or so with the release of 5150 we heard a different, more commercial side of Van Halen, which they did touch on, on the album 1984. So one version was just a bit \\"poppier\\" than the other. And the commercial success was a little higher with Hagar on board. Through all the years i never really knew or cared much what happened to Van Halen because frankly i just thought they blew it. I didn't even realize until about 2008 that Van Halen were still around, or that Eddie was even alive. They basically dropped off the face of the world. But now they have returned with one of their best releases to date. Hard to believe after a track like Tattoo. When i heard that i was less than impressed, but after that song, wow, it's just an endless punishment of heavy riffs, great drumming and bass playing (Wolfy is a real beast and IMO puts Michael Anthony to shame). Then again he is the son of Eddie Van Halen. So here are the songs track by track as most of you readers like to see. I will note before that Roth does sound great on most tracks, while struggles a little on others, but hey the guy is 56 years old and still sounds great.  1.Tattoo-No comment, watch the video, or skip it. Not sure why it's even on the album.  2.She's The Woman-One of the many great grooving songs on the album. Great wah wah pedal intro, good lyrics, and a great chorus that definitely soundsjust like the old days. Yes this was an old demo, but it sounds even better than the original. Roth's voice sounds a bit shot, but still good. (Sounds like something off of Van Halen II).  3.China Town-An amazing Steve Vai sounding intro that skews off into a great double bass \\"I'm The One\\" sounding song. Great chops from Alex. Roth's voice sounds a bit better on this track. Great solo from Eddie, just like Van Halen 1. Which this track sounds like it came from. Also the insane bass playing of Wolfgang can be heard greatly here, especially at the end.  4.Blood and Fire-A great radio vibe type song, that reminds you of \\"Dance The Night Away\\". And other parts that have riffs that sound like parts of Panama, after the chorus. So this is a mix of Van Halen II and 1984. Roth's voice sounds great on this track.  5.Bullethead-A total balls out, \\"On Fire\\" type track, minus the crazy Roth screams. Not much else to say, just a straight forward song with great riffs and solos. Decent Chorus.  6.As Is-Great drum and heavy guitar intro. Probably the heaviest on the album. Then the song takes off on a \\"Hot For Teacher\\" meets \\"Source Of Infection\\" speedy direction. Very good chorus, although the production is a little muddy on this one. So it's a bit hard to understand some of the lyrics. Great solos as usual. And the guys all singing together makes you not even really miss, Michael Anthony.  7.Honeybabysweetiedoll-Definitely not the song you would expect. It sounds very much like one of Joe Satriani's middle eastern solo songs. Eddie goes as far to even use the whammy pedal, for the high pitched notes. A tribute to Joe? Or jealousy? Perhaps (wink). Either way it's a good song, except Roth's talking can get a little tedious after a while. This is really a new direction for VH, so it's really hard to compare it to anything of old. Also one of the heaviest riffs on the album.  8.The Trouble With Never-Many are comparing this to an old Extreme song, with Nuno Betencourt. Because ofit's beginning blues riff. It does have it's similarities, but is it's own entity. Very much like a song from \\"Fair Warning\\" or \\"Women and Children First\\". Has a great classic sounding Halen chorus, although again Roth talking really adds nothing to the song.  9.Outta Space-Another straight ahead rocker that sounds like something from Van Halen 1 or II. Roth's voice shines on this track, i really think he's just getting it back into form. Not that he's really lost, he just needs to get back into the groove. Pretty simple song.  10.Stay Frosty-The most talked about song on the album as being Ice Cream Many part II. Which is what it is with much more mature lyrics. Even though i'm not really sure exactly what Dave's talking about. But when you listen to him talk in interviews your not really sure either. Great 50's type dance song, great guitar, and the classic VH old school rock ending.  11.Big River-One of the more radio friendly songs which i like a lot. Starts out with a great clean guitar, then goes off into a Running With The Devil bass groove rhythm. Sounds fresh and new although it could have been on an album from the Hagar years. He actually may have sounded better than Roth on it. Great song. Much more upbeat than the other tracks.  12.Beat's Workin'-Much more of a modern style VH song, with some old elements. And some great drum work from Alex. This is probably a clue to what the next VH album will sound like (if there is one). Roth really does struggle a bit to much on this track. Even though the chorus is catchy.  13.You And Your Blues-Accidently skipped this song, has the old classic VH sound with the usual blues laden licks from Eddie. Nothing really to special, although it is a very catchy tune.  If you have the special edition with the DVD acoustic sessions you'll get to enjoy, Panama, You And Your Blues, and Beautiful Girls, a nice little bonus. Although Roth looks like he's working for the railroad some of the things he does are pretty humorous. Andhe's definitely a show man, even when he's sitting down.  So VH have returned with a treat for the ears after nearly 30 years (without Diamond Dave). Some people like it, some people think it's to old school, which i thought that's what people wanted?? I for one am very happy to see some actual good music come out, where the guys \\"really\\" know how to play their instruments. I may only be 36, but it's time to show these young turds out there how to rock. And to those disrespecting Chickenfoot, oh well, i like them both. Sammy definitely can out sing roth and he's about 8 years older. Joe is a better song writer, and overall technical guitarist than Eddie, but then again, Eddie started it all. So A Different Kind Of Truth is a stellar album, especially in this day and age. So don't expect to get bored, because you definitely won't here. It's rocks, just as good as any of the old stuff. Later.

B0071T5PN0|5.0|I'm giving this 5 stars to encourage the band.  The album is not perfect.  It's overly compressed and too 'loud', in that EVERYTHING is up to 11 ALL THE TIME.  There are no dynamics whatsoever on this album, and the players and arrangements themselves don't see to be giving each other much room, which is unfortunate. That sort of production is what the mp3 marketplace dictates these days, though, and it doesn't surprise me that Van Halen played it 'safe' in this regard.  The 'invisible member' Ted Templeman, who gave them their sound in their hey-day, is sorely missed here, by me at least!<br /><br />**Addendum:  After listening to the album again with a good pair of headphones, I would tone down my criticism in this regard a good deal.  The production is growing on me.  The initial was my first impression through car speakers.<br /><br />And yes, I miss Michael Anthony too.  His spirit, playing, vocals and untold other contributions to the making of a Van Halen album are sorely missed.<br /><br />That having been said...<br /><br />This is the FIRST honest-to-goodness Van Halen album I've heard since '1984'.  I'm clearly Roth-biased, but the first Hagar album was inspired and I give him full props for that.  Cherone could have been great, but tried too hard in all the wrong directions, performance-wise, and just didn't have the songwriting chops and schtick that Roth has.  Nonetheless, his was the best and most versatile voice the group ever had, but in pop music, CHARACTER trounces quality every time and rightfully so.  Roth has character in spades, which is why he was always both tremendously successful AND exceedingly hard to work with!<br /><br />THAT having been said...<br /><br />WELCOME BACK VAN HALEN!  Wolfgang's chops on the bass sound truly inspired, and he's locking in with Alex's drumming much as well as his beloved predecessor, so cheers to him.  Eddie has some new sonic surprises on his guitar, even if none of them are likely to cause the sort of curiosity rush as his first forays into volume swelling pinch harmonic dive-bombing tapdom.<br /><br />As I said in the title, Roth's contributions here are very reminiscent of his Eat 'Em And Smile (which I love) album along with some of the darkness of Fair Warning.  There are no back-seat make-out anthems here or ode's to working mothers or whatever else it was that they were going on about all through the 90's.  Roth was as critical a part of the Van Halen sound as Lennon or McCartney was to that of the Beatles, and it is GREAT to have him back!  He's older, though, and not quite as poignant and funny as he used to be, lyric wise, and I could *REALLY* do without any more of the monotone rumbly raps.  SING for f***'s sake!  Find a melody and run with it.  Overall, the frequent torrents of words work in terms of musical alliteration, but could stand a serious paring down for impact and clarity. I say, if the lyric doesn't merit shouting or singing from the roof-tops, then chuck it, but that's me.<br /><br />Alex's drumming is over the top in his usual enjoyable and inimitable style, and he seems to lock in with Wolfgang very nicely.  I hope what I'm hearing there (in terms of their locking in together) is real, and not just something stitched together on the timeline with a mouse in Pro Tools!  A live performance will tell the tale in that regard.<br /><br />I have kids of my own, and while I really think Michael Anthony should be in there instead, I can perfectly respect Eddie's enthusiasm for getting his son involved.  Wolfgang also locks in some very sophisticated bass lines with some of his father's noodling, much as Billy Sheehan did with Steve Vai on Eat 'em And Smile.  It's done to great effect here and shows that these players are looking forward and not just sitting back to re-create a patented or tried-and-true 'sound'.  They're exploring and having some fun, but doing it on their own terms. B0061QD82E|5.0|If it weren't an animated feature, and was a contemporary live action romantic comedy/drama, Lady and the Tramp could very well star, for instance, Jake Gyllenhaal or Ryan Reynolds with Reese Witherspoon or Jennifer Aniston.<br /><br />\\"She's a well-bred, meticulous looker but a little naive and a bit of a control freak! He's a sloppy, jaunty hunk who lives on a lots of friends' couches and has trouble committing to one person! Over the course of the movie, she learns to lighten up and he learns the value of a responsible relationship! A popular song plays as they head back to the city from Central Park or hop on the cable car with the Golden Gate bridge in the distance! Or any number of variations on the same story!\\"<br /><br />Even though it was released over 50 years ago and occasionally betrays its era (the baby bottles in the window, stereotyped incidental characters that were \\"safer\\" in their day than now), Lady and the Tramp is, in many ways, more sophisticated, witty and -- dare I say -- sexier than some of today's wafer-thin incarnations.<br /><br />Come on, that spaghetti scene. It predates the eating scene in Tom Jones by several years. Lady and Tramp awake in the morning after a night out, followed by a scene in which her neighbor dogs Jock and Trusty propose marriage to her. Sure, it's so she has a roof over her head (the annoying Aunt Sarah has put her outside) but it suggests they're trying to make her an honest woman.<br /><br />I thought I was really stretching things by suggesting that last assumption, so you can imagine my surprise when several Disney artists and historians say just about the same thing on one of the bonus features! Movies were changing in 1955, and Walt might not have made Lady and the Tramp in quite the same way had the war not prevented it from going into production a decade or so sooner.<br /><br />All underlying meanings aside, Lady and the Tramp is one of Disney's biggest consistent crowd pleasers, as is evidenced by the fact that this supposedly \\"old\\" movie is neck in neck with the latest Twilight movie for the number one sales spot (as if this writing the Blu-ray has edged out its rival). The story is brisk, relatable (the idea of being \\"replaced\\" in someone's heart worked so well for Toy Story, too) and it is visually stunning. Everything has a handsome sheen on it, capturing a Main Street, U.S.A. idyll that Walt was simultaneously creating for his Disneyland Park.<br /><br />On the new diamond edition Blu-ray, this detail and color are nothing short of breathtaking -- even in seemingly simple scenes like one in which Lady walks upstairs and various carpet and wallpaper patterns go off in maddening directions, yet perfectly in perspective. It's the Alice in Wonderland look, but sane.<br /><br />Perhaps no other Disney animated film features a larger cast of iconic voice talents who had shone in radio and were moving into television, including Grammy winning comedy giant Stan Freberg as the Beaver (Freberg shows us how he does the whistle voice in a bonus feature).<br /><br />Alan Reed, soon to become immortalized as the original Fred Flintstone, is Boris the Russian wolfhound. Verna Felton, who would grace many a Disney feature, moving effortlessly from villainy to benevolence, would soon be Fred's mother-in-law. Dal McKennon (Gumby, The Archie Show, Epcot's American Adventure) plays several roles, one that sounds much like his Mr. Weatherbee at Riverdale High.<br /><br />And doing the most voices of all, almost heard in every scene, is the underrated Bill Thompson, whose most famous voice embodied the White Rabbit and Jock for Disney, as well as Droopy for MGM and Touch&eacute; Turtle for Hanna-Barbera.<br /><br />This is also the first Disney animated feature with a starring lead. Before Billy Joel, Elton John or Phil Collins became part of Disney projects, pop goddess Peggy Lee was brought aboard to add a creative imprint unlike anything Walt had ever so graciously welcomed.<br /><br />Even though Lady and the Tramp isn't a musical in the traditional sense, Lee's presence is felt throughout, either through songs she wrote with Sonny Burke or any of her four voices (the breakout being the torchy Peg, who's a cross between Mae West and Jimmy Cagney.<br /><br />As per the usual custom, most of the classic DVD features from the 2006 Platinum Edition DVD have been moved to the Blu-ray. The Blu-ray now has an Audio Commentary (thank you!) and the nifty \\"second screen\\" feature that allows you to gather further behind the scenes treasures from your laptop while the disc plays on your player. There's also a deleted song that Tramp was going to sing called \\"Free as the Breeze.\\"<br /><br />By the way, if you're interested in such wonderful songs that were deleted from Disney classics, you'll want to check out Russell Schroeder's superb, illustrated Disney's Lost Chords, Volume One and Volume Two.ither through songs she wrote with Sonny Burke or any of her four voices (the breakout being the torchy Peg, who's a cross between Mae West and Jimmy Cagney. B002EWD0I6|5.0|LARK RISE TO CANDLEFORD is The Beeb's answer to such dramas as \\"Little House on the Prairie\\" and \\"Road to Avonlea\\".  Based on several autobiographical volumes from Flora Thompson, the series is a gentle echo of a long-vanished time when community spirit and family values weren't so hard to come by as they seem to be now.<br /><br />Concerned about the future of their eldest daughter Laura (Olivia Hallinan) in their small village of Lark Rise, Robert and Emma Timmins (Brendan Coyle and Claudie Blakley) arrange for the girl to go to work at the post office in the neighbouring market town of Candleford.  The post office and adjoining forge are both run with acute precision by Emma's cousin, Dorcas Lane (Julia Sawalha), one of the town's leading personalities.  As Laura literally steps into an entirely new world, she learns all about the lives of the Candleford residents on her daily mail run.<br /><br />Spinster sisters Pearl and Ruby Pratt (Matilda Ziegler and Victoria Hamilton) run the town's most fashionable shop and are as nosey and snobbish as they are full of long-buried family scandals.  Caroline Arless (Dawn French) from Lark Rise is always getting into trouble with debt-collectors because of her reckless spending habits, whilst Laura's childhood romance with Caroline's son Alf (John Dagleish) soon evaporates because of Laura's busy schedule.  The twin communities of Lark Rise and Candleford are connected not only through the post office, but by local magistrate Sir Timothy Midwinter (Ben Miles) and his wife Lady Adelaide (Olivia Grant).  Their marriage is troubled by Adelaide's feverish quest to have a baby and the unresolved adolescent courtship of Dorcas and Timothy, which Adelaide increasingly regards as a direct threat to her happiness.<br /><br />The performances are perfection.  Her earlier roles in \\"Pride & Prejudice\\" and \\"Cranford\\" notwithstanding, who would have ever pegged Julia Sawalha (\\"clinically-depressed budgerigar\\" Saffy from \\"Absolutely Fabulous\\") as the prim leading lady of a weekly period drama series?  Dorcas Lane is the pivot from which most of the drama unfolds in this series and she more than holds her own as one of the great heroines.  I adore her.
